Summary 0:
EU rules on collective investment undertakings (UCITS)
EU rights and obligations under UCITS
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1286/2014 — rules and obligations of collective investment undertaking investors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that investors who invest in collective investments in transferable securities, such as mutual funds, do not lose out on the benefits of the EU’s single market.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules for the management and supervision of UCITS.
It applies to the management, supervision and control of collective investments* (CITs) and to the marketing of marketing communications to investors.
UCITS may be externally or internally managed, depending on its legal form.
EU countries must ensure that:
all UCITS are regulated by the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and that they comply with the relevant EU rules and regulations;
the relevant national authorities have the powers to take appropriate measures to ensure the proper functioning of the UCITS market;
EU-wide rules are in place to ensure a high level of protection of investors’ rights and ensure that UCITS investors receive the relevant information they need to make informed investment decisions;
investors have the right to receive a key investor information document (KIID) and a key information document for each UCITS before investing in a UCITS, and to be informed of any changes to their rights or obligations.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLACE INTO FORCE?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The UCITS Regulation (EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
For more information, see:
‘UCITS regulation’ on the European Commission's website
KEY TERMS
* Collective investment undertaking: an investment undertaking that is managed by an EU country.
* KEY INFORMATION DOCUMENT
EU country: the EU country in which the collective investments are managed.

Summary 1:
EU market surveillance — enforcing EU competition law
EU competition law — enforcement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — market surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ market surveillance authorities (market surveillance authorities) carry out market surveillance to ensure compliance with EU competition rules.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that market surveillance is effective, proportionate and dissuasive in order to ensure fair competition in the EU market for goods and services.
The regulation lays down rules for market surveillance.
It applies to all EU legislation on competition, including harmonisation of EU harmonisation legislation.
Market surveillance authorities
Each EU country must designate its own market surveillance authority.
They are responsible for:
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on market surveillance legislation;
monitoring and analysing the compliance of the legislation and ensuring that it is complied with;
carrying out joint activities to promote compliance;
organising and carrying out administrative cooperation between the authorities;
coordinating with the relevant economic operators.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
    ъ  тараканскалка баккародна гаркашка   бълъбанакодкаганкакраннаъкцакшанодърнанънчакинаблакгоданданикогакна  гъцъантанграчка
For more information, see:
‘Market surveillance’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Market surveillance: the process by which EU countries collect, analyse, analyse and disseminate information on the compliance with the EU’s competition law.

Summary 2:
Licensing of copyright and related rights for retransmission of television and radio programmes and related content (recast)
Licensing and licensing of copyright & related content for broadcasting and other related content
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/33/EC on licensing and licensing the licensing of certain retransmissions of copyright, related rights and related subject matter for television or radio programmes
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It lays down rules for the licensing, licensing and distribution of certain rights for the broadcasting of copyright-related content and related material.
It aims to simplify the licensing process and to ensure fair competition in the field of copyright.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the broadcasting and transmission of television programmes originating in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
the broadcasting of radio programmes originating outside the EU;
the transmission of films, television programmes and other protected subject matter originating in other EU countries;
programmes originating in non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1), Ireland and the United States (2);
the distribution of radio and television programmes produced in the UK (3), Ireland (4), France (5), Germany (6) and Switzerland (7).
EU countries may provide for arrangements for obtaining authorisation from the rightholders.
The rights holders must receive appropriate remuneration for the retransribution of their works and subject matter.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 January 2010. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive in 2009.
For more information, see:
Copyright and licensing (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Copyright: the right to reproduce, reproduce, adapt, adapt or adapt a work for the purpose of making it available to the public.
* Related material: the content of a film, a television programme or a radio programme, or other protected material, which is produced, adapted, adapted or adapted for the purposes of making available on the market.
ACT
 Directive (EU) 2017/852 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2017 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the licensing and the distribution of copyright or related material for broadcasting or other related material (OJ L 347, 12.12.2017, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
 
Directives 2009/34/EC and 2009/35/EC of the Court of Justice of the EU and of Justice (EC) No 596/2009 on the licensing (recasting) of copyright (OCT) and related materials for broadcasting, and repealing Directives 2004/17/EC, 2004/18/EC (EC), 2004/19/EC) and 2004/20/EC.
Directions 2004/24/EC concerning the distribution, transmission and reception of copyright material for television, radio and internet services and Directive 2004/23/EC relating to copyright (Directive 2004/22/EC), 2005/24-EC and 2006/23-EC concerning copyright (directive 2006/25/EC).
DirectIVE 2009/31/EC concerns only the exercise of certain copyright and rights for certain retrunmission rights to the extent necessary to simplify licensing of such services and with regard to programmes originating from other Member States. The directive does not concern the licensing or distribution of other rights for such services.
Last updated: 28.08.2017



Summary 3:
The.eu.eu TLD — good governance of the.eu domain name registrar
The.EU.euTLD – good governance and good governance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/679 on the.EU TLD
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the governance and transparency of the EU’s online domain name (.eu) registry.
It sets out the rules and procedures for the creation of a registry for.eu domains.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out rules for the establishment of a.eu registrar.
The purpose of the registry is to ensure that.eu names are available to all interested parties, subject to availability and availability.
To this end, the regulation lays down a number of conditions for the registry, including that:
the registry must be open to all applicants for domain names;
it must be free of charge;
the registrars must be accredited by the European Commission;
there must be a multistakeholder advisory group set up by the Commission to advise the Commission on good governance;
registers must comply with EU law and the Charter of the European Union.
Applicants must be able to use a domain name that is not already registered in another EU country and that does not fall within the scope of this regulation.
Registry
The registry is managed by a registrar established in an EU country. The registrar is responsible for managing the registry and ensuring that it is free of any conflicts of interest and that might arise.
There are 2 types of registrar:
a.eu accredited registrar that provides the same services as accredited.eu Registrars that provide equivalent services.
A.eu registered registrar must:
provide the Commission with all the information it needs to manage the registry;
ensure that the information on the WHOIS database is not excessive in relation to the database;
notify the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of any changes to the registry's rules or procedures;
keep a publicly accessible database of all domain names that are registered in.eu;
apply the same conditions as those laid down in the regulation for accredited.EU registered registraries that provide comparable services;
use the same terms and conditions for all.eu-registered domain names.
In addition, the Commission may request information from the registry to that end.
Role of the Commission
The Commission is assisted by a multisectoral advisory group composed of representatives of stakeholders that are established in the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Domain name: a term used to refer to a web address, e.g. ‘internet address’.
* Registry: a body responsible for registering domain names in the top-level domain (TLD).
*.eu: the top level domain name, which is reserved for the purpose of providing a single online address.
REFERENCES

Summary 4:
European Common Foreign and Security Fund (CEF) — EU support for the EU’s common foreign and security policy (CFSP) (2014-2020) (European Commission).
The European Common Foreign & Security Fund is a European Union (EU) instrument designed to support the common security and defence policy (CSDP).
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 596/2014/EU of 20 December 2014 establishing the European Common European Foreign and security Fund (the European Common Europe Fund).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The CEF supports the common defence and security strategy (CEFP), which aims to support EU countries’ efforts to fight terrorism and the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMD).
KEY POINTS
Through the CEF, the EU aims to:
contribute to the development of a common EU security policy;
promote the sustainable development of security and stability in Europe;
support the efforts of EU countries to tackle terrorism;
improve the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms;
encourage cooperation between EU countries in the field of defence;
provide financial support to EU countries that are involved in the fight against terrorism.
Scope
The EU supports projects in the fields of:
energy;
counter-terrorism;
human rights and security;
environmental protection;
military training;
civil defence; and
military research and development;
security and defence cooperation;
transport;
information society;
economic development.
Specific eligibility rules apply to the adaptation of the TEN-T to civilian-defence dual use;
projects;
rehabilitation of displaced persons;
research and innovation;
training of civil servants;
employment and social inclusion;
education, training and training of social workers;
health and well-being;
science and technology research;
development of new technologies;
integration of renewable energy;
sustainable transport;
energy efficiency;
climate change.
Funding
The fund is funded by the EU budget and is managed by the European Investment Bank for Europe (EIBE) and the European Regional Development Fund (EDFIF).
It is managed and administered by the Commission.
The Commission is responsible for implementing the work programmes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Decision 2014/975/EU has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The common security policy is one of the EU's main instruments for fighting terrorism. It is designed to help EU countries fight against the spread of WMD.
For more information, see:
‘The Common Security and Defence Policy’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Cross-border projects: projects involving more than one EU country.
* Civil defence: projects designed to contribute to the prevention, detection, investigation, investigation and prosecution of terrorism and related crimes.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Resolution of 21 December 2014 on the establishment of the European common European European Fund for the Common Defence and Security (the CEF) (OJ L 347, 28.12.2014, pp. 1-2)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 5:
Internet hosting service providers
Internet hosting services providers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — hosting service provider obligations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the legal certainty and security of hosting services in the online environment.
It lays down rules for hosting services to ensure the protection of users’ privacy and the protection against illegal content.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all hosting services, including video-sharing platform providers. It does not apply to the hosting of audio-sharing platforms.
Hosting service providers must:
ensure that the content they host is protected against unlawful interference with users‘ personal data and personal data;
protect the privacy of users and the security of their personal data when hosting content;
provide users with a clear and effective way of reporting illegal content to the competent authorities.
Providers must ensure that:
the content is protected and that users can easily access it;
users have the right to access the content and information they need to make a complaint;
the hosting provider is responsible for ensuring that the information they provide is protected;
there is a contact point for users to make complaints about illegal content and for the removal of illegal content from the site.
The hosting provider must also ensure that users have access to a complaint form and that the contact point is accessible to them. The contact point must include information about the language in which the complaint can be addressed and the language of the complaint must be used.
the contact point should also be able to provide information on:
where a complaint has been made, the hosting provider’s contact details;
where an offence has been committed, the host service provider must take appropriate action;
in the event of a breach of the protection provided for in the regulation, the user must be informed of the consequences of the breach and the measures taken;
when a complaint is made, users must be given the opportunity to lodge a complaint with the competent authority of the EU country where the offence was committed.
In addition, EU countries must establish a monitoring programme to monitor the implementation of the regulation. This monitoring programme must include:
monitoring of content and related data; and
the collection of data and other necessary evidence.
Monitoring programme
EU countries must also set up an EU-wide monitoring programme. It must be updated every 2 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Hosting services’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EU) No 550/2004 of 20 December 2004 on the hosting services regulation (OJ L 183, 31.12.2004, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 555/2004 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  (1)
Report from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the application of Regulation (EEC, EC) No 530/2004 on the protection services regulation on the Internet (COM(2006) 696 final, 28.3.2006)
last update 14.02.2016



Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027 (FPR&I 2021-27)
Framework Program for Research & Innovation 2021–2027
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2021/2027 on Horizon Europe
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets out how the EU will invest in R&I through Horizon Europe.
It aims to deliver scientific, technological, economic, environmental and societal impact in pursuit of this general objective and to maximise the added value of the EU’s investments in research and innovation.
KEY POINTS
Through Horizon Europe, the EU aims to:
increase the number of researchers and technological innovators in Europe;
improve the quality of research;
contribute to the creation of jobs and economic growth;
encourage innovation and creativity.
The programme is funded by the European Union (EU).
Funding
The EU will provide €2.8 billion for the period 2021–27. This amount includes €1.2 billion from Horizon Europe and €1 billion from the Creative Europe Programme.
EU funding
The European Union will provide up to €2 billion for Horizon Europe in the form of grants and loans.
This amount includes:
€1.5 billion for research and development (R&D) in the fields of science and technology (i.e. basic and applied research);
€2.3 billion for R&D in the field of culture and creative policies;
€3.7 billion for cultural and creative policy research.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The decision has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Framework Programme is part of the broader EU Horizon 2020 programme.
For more information, see:
‘Horizon Europe’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Research and innovation (R & I): the process of discovering, developing and exploiting new knowledge and technologies.
ACT
Commission Implementing Decision 2011/852/EU of 21 December 2011 on Horizon European 2021-2020 (OJ L 183, 28.12.2011, pp. 1–2)
RELATED ACTS
Commission Decision (EC) No 596/2013 of 29 June 2013 on the Framework Programme for research & innovation 2021-2019 (Oj L 183 of 28.6.2013, p. 1)

Summary 7:
EU satellite communications programme
EU satellite communication programme
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/852 — EU satellite communications (GOVSATCOM) programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
 
It aims to support the European Union’s (EU's) Galileo and EGNOS (European Geostationary Navigation Satellite Communications (GNSS) programmes.
KEY POINTS
The programme aims to:
ensure that EU satellite communication services are interoperable, interoperable and standardised;
promote interoperability and interoperability between EU countries’ satellite communication systems (GPSs);
provide the European Commission with information and communication technology (ICT) services to the public and private sectors;
support the development and deployment of EU-wide GPSs;
improve the availability of GPS services to users in the EU and to the private sector.
The GOVSatCOM programme is funded by the EU's general budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
  
The EU has been involved in the Galileo programme since 2013.
For more information, see:
European Galileo programme (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* GPSSatellite communication services: a set of satellite communications services designed to provide the public with information, communication and information on the Galileo satellite system and its functions, such as the Galileo Galileo satellite positioning system, the European Galileo satellite navigation system and the European GNSSDATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Fund (EDIF) 2019/2027 — calls for proposals and tenders for the creation of European Digital Innovation Hubs (EDIH)
European digital innovation fund (EDIFF)2019/2028 — calls to tender and calls for applications for the establishment of European digital innovation hubs (EDIU)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2020/2093 setting up the European Union’s (EU) European Innovation Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE PROGRAMMECISION?
The decision establishes a European Union (EU), Euratom, multiannual programme to support the development of innovative, high-quality and high-skilled ICT skills and competencies in Europe.
The aim of the programme is to:
promote the development and use of ICT competencies and skills in Europe;
encourage innovation and entrepreneurship;
support the creation and development of European innovation hubs;
contribute to the growth and competitiveness of the European digital economy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme is open to all EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) Member States.
It is funded by the European Commission.
Objectives
The objective of the EDF is to support:
the creation of a European digital Innovation Hub (EDH) in each Member State;
the development of high-performing ICT-intensive enterprises;
innovation and entrepreneurship in the areas such as:
cybersecurity and trust;
artificial intelligence (AI);
automation;
technologies;
digitalisation;
health and safety;
information society
science and technology (i.e. the use of information and communication technologies (ICTs);
digital education and training;
education and training.
Specific objectives
The EDF supports the following specific objectives:
creating a European Innovation Hub for each specific objective;
raising awareness of the need for ICT specialists;
promoting entrepreneurship and innovation;
improving the quality of life of people and the environment;
developing a culture of innovation.
Funding
The European Commission may grant up to €2.5 billion to specific objectives, including €1 billion for specific objectives such as cybersecurity and trust, and €2 billion for artificial intelligence.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Both decisions have applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘European Innovation Fund’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
 
Decisions 2010/125/EC, 2010/126/EC and 2010/127/EC of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States of the Union establishing the European Digital Investment Fund (EIF) and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 596/2009 (OJ L 183, 31.7.2010, pp. 1-8)

Summary 9:
European Union programme for market surveillance and market surveillance (EUROPEAN MARKET SECURITY AND MARKET PROTECTION PROGRAMME (EMPSP)
European Union (EMSP)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2016/852 on the European Union’s Market Surveillance and Market Surveillance Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It aims to improve the protection of consumers and businesses against market failures in the internal market.
KEY POINTS
The EMSP is the main instrument for combating market failures and distortions of competition in the EU.
It is designed to support actions for the development of relevant information on markets, including the publication of the EU consumer scoreboards and the creation of joint market surveillance campaigns.
The programme is funded by the EU budget.
Objectives
The main objectives of the EMPSP are to:
improve consumer protection;
improve market surveillance;
promote sustainable food production and consumption;
tackle food waste and combating food fraud;
encourage sustainable production, consumption and supply chains;
support small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in the food sector;
contribute to the fight against poverty and social exclusion;
ensure that consumers have access to quality and affordable products;
fight against social exclusion.
In addition, the EMSP supports actions for:
the development of the SME Envoy Network;
the creation of a European consumer scoreboard;
combating social exclusion and the social exclusion of consumers from the market;
strengthening the role of the European Commission;
informing consumers about the importance of the programme;
raising awareness of the benefits of EMSPPolicies and measures to improve consumer protection.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
EMSP (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Decisions (EU, Euratom) 2016-852 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States, meeting within the Council of 21 June 2016 on the EU Market Surveillance programme and repealing Council Decision (EC) No 596/2006 (OJ L 183, 29.7.2016, pp. 1-8)

Summary 10:
EU rules on the import and export of firearms and ammunition for self-defence and self-inflicted injuries
EU controls on imports and exports of handguns and assault rifles
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/856 — rules on importing and exporting certain firearms, ammunition and ammunition
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to protect EU citizens and the general public against the illegal trade in handguns and ammunition.
It codifies and repeals Directive 91/477/EEC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to the import of handguns, assault rifles, semi-automatic firearms, shotguns, revolvers, carbines and pistols, as well as to ammunition. It does not apply to transfers regulated by Directive 2009/43/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary).
It does not cover:
transport of firearms between EU countries;
the transfer of firearms intended for use in the EU;
transfer of ammunition intended for the use of the EU.
EU countries must ensure that:
the import of firearms is accompanied by an authorisation issued by the competent authority of the country in which the firearms are to be imported;
there is no fee or charge for the transfer of the firearms;
personal data are deleted from the data-filing systems.
National authorities are responsible for the proper storage arrangements for the firearms and for the ammunition they acquire and possess.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Firearms’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Firearms: handguns, rifles and shotguns, shotguns and carbines;
explosives and launchers: explosive military missiles and launchers;
convertible weapons: rifles, shotguns or pistols that have been converted from automatic firearms.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2011/722/EU of 21 December 2011 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the transfer and import of certain firearms and of ammunition (codified version) (OJ L 183, 28.12.2011, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
 
Directives 2009/42/EC and 2009/47/EC on the registration of firearms, and on the transfer, importation, export and possession of ammunition and repealing Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 (OCT) No 652/2004) (Directive 2011/13/EU on the control of the import, export, possession and transfer of ammunition, and repealing Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 1096/2005 and (EC), No 1097/2006 (Directives 2011/23/EU and 2011/24/EU) (See summary)
Commission Implementing Decision 2011/12/EU establishing a European Firearms Information System (EASIS) for the storage, transport, import and sale of firearms. This system allows the Commission to collect and analyse data on the number and category of firearms imported into the EU and the types of ammunition that are intended for their intended use. It also provides for the exchange of information on the types and categories of ammunition for which the ammunition is intended.
Council Regulation (EEC, Euratom) No 796/2006 on the protection of the public from the threat of terrorism and the prevention of acts of violence against the security of the population (OJC) (Council Directive 2013/11/EU). See summary.

Summary 11:
Reporting obligations and formalities in the field of maritime transport — EU rules for maritime transport
Reporting obligations, formalities and reporting obligations in the fields of transport and customs
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — EU maritime reporting obligations and reporting formalities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down common rules for the reporting obligations of EU countries’ maritime transport authorities.
It aims to improve the efficiency of the EU’s maritime transport system (MTS).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Norway and Switzerland.
EU countries must ensure that:
the maritime national single windows (NMSs) are set up for each EU country;
the NMSs are interoperable with each other and with other EU-wide systems;
there is a single location database to facilitate ship reporting;
all EU countries are required to ensure the availability of the NMS database data to the maritime National Single Windows for facilitation of ship reporting.
The Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The delegated regulation applies from 15 August 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
Maritime transport is the backbone of trade and communications within and beyond the single market.
For more information, see:
‘Reporting obligations’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 12:
Information exchange system (EETS) for failing to pay a road fee and on vehicles that fail to comply with a toll system
Information exchange mechanism (EATS) for not paying a road toll
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/413 — system for exchanging information on those who fail to pay road fees and on their vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the exchange of information on road-safety-related traffic offences between EU countries.
It recasts and repeals Directives (EC) No 596/2004 and (ECB/2004) on road tolls.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must set up an electronic toll system (e.g. EETS) to exchange information on the number of vehicles registered in another EU country and the number and type of vehicles involved in such offences.
The EETS system is designed to be interoperable with the EU’s traffic offences database.
EETS providers must ensure that the information they provide is accurate, up-to-date and comparable to that provided by national traffic offences databases.
They must also ensure that EETS providers are entitled to be remunerated by the toll charger.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive applies from 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND

Toll: a fee charged by a toll charger for the transport of goods between 2 EU countries, i.e. the cost of which is paid by the driver of the vehicle to the toll collector.

Summary 14:
EU-wide biometric identity cards for the protection of personal data and the right of free movement of persons
EU–EU biometric identification cards for protecting personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/849 on the European Union’s electronic identity cards and residence documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU citizens and their family members have access to a single EU-wide identity card and residence document.
It lays down rules on the security features and technical specifications for these cards.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality. It does not apply to non-EU nationals.
EU countries must ensure that the same security features are used by all EU countries when issuing the cards and that they are compatible with each other.
National authorities must:
ensure that the card is secure and that it can be read by machines used by EU countries other than those which issue the cards;
ensures that the information on the card and on the document is stored in a secure and easily accessible database;
provide a contact point for each EU country where the card can be used;
identify the holder of the card at all points of entry and exit from the EU.
The card must be issued by the national authority responsible for issuing the card or by the competent national authority in the EU country in which it is issued.
Each EU country must provide the competent authorities with the necessary information to issue the card, such as:
the name and address of the contact point;
the date of birth and the date of naturalisation or naturalisation of the holder;
a description of the identity card or residence document;
any additional security features that may be added to the card; and
the number and duration of the validity of the cards or residence documents.
In addition, EU countries may provide for additional technical specifications to improve the security and the ease of use of the EU-issued identity card.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
EU citizens have the right to have an identity card issued to them by their country of residence.
For more information, see:
European Union biometric cards (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* European Union identity card: a card issued by an EU country to a person who is a national of another EU country.
* EU residence document: a residence document issued by a country which is a member of the European Economic Area (EEA).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/749 of 21 December 2018 on the common European Union system of identity cards (OJ L 347, 28.12.2018, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Council Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 of 29 June 2013 on the harmonisation of national laws and regulations relating to the recognition and recognition of biometric identifiers in the field of criminal matters and repealing Council Regulation (EEC, EC) No 652/2004 (OCT) No 1296/2004).
Council Decision 2014/856/EU of 27 June 2014 on the application of Regulation (EU), EC, Euronews, Euromonitor, Euromoney, Eurizon, Eurometrics and the European Commission Implementing Regulation (EMU) No 182/2011 on the verification of biometrics in criminal matters.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management
INTEROPERABLE INFORMATION SYSTEMS FOR SECURITY, BORDER AND MIGRATION MANAGEMENT
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2016/1624 on the interoperability of the EU Information System for Security, Border and Migration (eu-LISA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU), EU countries and the European Commission have interoperable information systems (e.g. the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (eBCA), the European Customs and Border Protection Agency (ECBPA) and Europol) for border and maritime security and migration.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the responsibilities of EU countries, the Commission, the European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPSB) and the EU institutions and bodies in relation to the implementation of the decision.
EU countries are responsible for:
developing and using the eBCA and the ECBPA;
ensuring that the EBCA and the ECPPA are up-to-date and interoperable;
providing the competent national authorities with the information they need to carry out their tasks;
assisting the Commission with the development and operation of the Europol and Eurojust information systems.
The Commission is responsible for ensuring that:
the eBACA and eCBPA are interoperable and that the relevant national authorities have access to the relevant information;
Europol and the relevant relevant national databases;
the competent national databases are available to the Commission and the Commission;
national authorities have the necessary information to ensure the proper functioning of the systems;
there is a clear separation of responsibilities between the different information systems; and
there are clear rules on the use of personal data.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
From 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission launched a communication in December 2016 on interoperability between the information systems in the field of security and border management.
For more information, see:
‘Implementing interoperability’ on Europol’s website.

Summary 16:
Renewable energy — energy efficiency and energy efficiency (REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Directive (EU) 2017/852
11.12.2017
-
OJ L 183 of 10.11.2017.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It aims to improve the efficiency of the EU’s electricity market by reducing greenhouse gas emissions (GHG emissions) from renewable energy sources.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that the baseline share of renewable energy in their electricity generation is at least 50% by 2020 compared to that of fossil fuels;
establish a renewable energy share of at least 75% by 2023 compared to the baseline for the EU as a whole;
set up a national renewable energy target for 2030.
The baseline share must not be lower than 75% compared to 1990 levels. EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure compliance with that baseline share.
National renewable energy targets must be set out in Annex II to the directive.
EU Member States must ensure that:
the baseline share is not less than 75%;
the renewable energy source is not used more than 50% of the total electricity generated;
the EU-wide renewable energy goal is not lower than 80% by 2030;
there is no obligation for Member States to reduce their baseline share below 75%.
Member States must also ensure that renewable energy is used only where it is necessary to meet the minimum GHG emission reduction targets.
In addition of renewables to the energy mix for electricity generation for the outermost regions is made up of a large proportion of fuel oil, which is a major source of GHG emissions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of energy efficiency measures to tackle climate change. These include:
energy efficiency targets for 2030 (see summary);
carbon capture and storage (CCS) targets for 2050 (see overview);
the CO2 emissions reduction targets for 2023 and 2035% by 2050.
For more information, see:
‘Clean energy’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Renewable energy source: any energy source that can be used to generate, store, process, transport, store or transmit electricity.
ACT
Entry Into force: transposition into force of Directive 2009/36/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2009 on the approximation of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions of Member States relating to renewable energy (recast) (Official Journal C 202 of 20.12-20.2010)
RELATED ACTS
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 of the General Assembly of 13 December 2008 on the harmonisation of the law on the production, transmission and distribution of electricity and repealing Council Directive 93/29/EEC (OJ C 202, 20.11-20).
  This regulation lays down the rules for the harmonization of the rules on the generation, transmission, distribution and sale of electricity in the European Union (EEC) and on the sale of energy to third parties (Directive 2009/35/EC).
Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 652/2008 and (EC), EC, EFTA and EFTA have been amended several times, most recently by Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1369.
Regulating (EU), EC and EC countries must set up a system for the collection and transmission of information on renewable energy from third parties. This information must include the quantity and price of the energy involved, the type of energy used, the energy source and the type and quantity of renewable resources used.
This information must be sent to the Commission at least every 2 years.

Summary 17:

Access to number and service providers
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 on the right to number portability
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve competition in the EU’s electronic communications market by making it easier for consumers to use numbers from different national numbering plans.
It recasts and repeals Directives (EC) No 596/2004 and (EC, EC, EC) No 652/2004.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the following services:
personal communications services using numbers from national or international numbering schemes;
telecommunications services using public numbering plans;
internet services using publicly assigned numbering resources.
National numbering plans
EU countries must ensure that:
national numbering plans do not discriminate between EU and non-EU countries;
the national plans are open to competition;
national plans are free from administrative charges;
services are free of charge;
any charges are reasonable and transparent;
there is no discrimination based on nationality, religion, race, colour, ethnic origin, language, language or sexual orientation.

Internet services: internet-based services that allow users to send, receive, store, transfer, store or transfer data using a number from a national numbering plan.
Online services: online services that provide access to a number, such as email addresses, postal addresses, telephone numbers and postal addresses.

Summary 20:
Energy efficiency labels — reducing energy consumption and carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions
Energy consumption and CO2 emissions
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — energy efficiency labels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It lays down rules for the use of energy-efficient labels on products intended for sale in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation aims to ensure that labels on energy efficiency products sold in the EU are clearly visible and clearly distinguishable from other products on the market.
It does not apply to products intended to be sold in non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the label is easily recognisable, simple and concise.
Labels must include the following information:
the energy efficiency class of the product;
the number of units of the model in which the product falls into the top two energy efficiency classes A and B and further technological development can be expected;
a description of how the product is designed to reduce its energy consumption;
how the product complies with the EU’s 2030 climate and energy policy framework.
The label must be clearly visible on the product packaging and on the packaging itself.
A product database must be established to monitor compliance with the regulation.
Manufacturers and importers must provide the Commission with a product database containing information on the energy efficiency of the products they place on the EU market. The Commission must review the label with a view to rescaling if it estimates that:
30 % of the units of models belonging to a product group sold within the Union market fall within the top energy efficiency category A or B, or if 50 % of units fall into either class A or class B, but further technological progress is expected; or
50 % or more units of a model belonging to the same product group sell within the EU.
If the Commission considers that a product does not conform to the requirements of the regulation, it may further rescale the label.
Importers and distributors must not display the label on products which do not comply with the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/853 has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Energy efficiency’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Energy efficiency label: a label on a product which is clearly visible to consumers.
ACT
Commission Delegation (EU), Euratom of 12 December 2019, on the introduction of a new energy efficiency label for products designed for sale within the Community (OJ L 347, 14.12.2019, pp. 1-2)
RELATED ACTS
Regulation (EC) No 852/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2013 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the sale of energy efficient products and repealing Council Regulation (EC, EC, EC) No 652/2008 (OCT) No 1252/2009) (See amending act(s) No 1/2013)
Regulations (EC), EC, EFTA and EFTA) No 596/2013, (ECE) No 1069/2013 and (ECB) No 1169/2014 of the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP) and Directives 2014/35/EU, 2014/34/EU and 2014/36/EU of the International Energy Agency (IEA) and the International Labour Organisation (ILO)

Summary 21:
Protecting personal data
Protecting human data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — protection of personal data and the right to data security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that the protection of the personal data of natural persons is respected by EU countries’ data protection authorities.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
natural persons have access to their personal data;
they have the right of access to the processing and storage of their data; and
they retain their data for at least 5 years after the data has been processed.
The regulation applies to all EU countries and to non-EU countries.
It does not apply to the EU’s single market for goods and services (e.g. the EU countries that are not part of the European Union (EU).
It also applies to EU countries which are not party to the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) and which have not yet ratified it.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Data protection and data security’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Personal data: data on a person, such as their name, date of birth, gender, place of residence, nationality or place of employment.

  — Protection of the rights and fundamental rights and freedoms of the natural persons and their right to privacy

Summary 22:
Harmonised index for the harmonisation of the prices of goods and services (HICP) in the European Union
Harmonomically-harmonised indices for the Harmonised index on the price of products and services
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — harmonised indices on the cost of living
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the price index for goods (the Harmonised Indices for Consumer Prices).
It is designed to help the European Commission and the European Central Bank (ECB) in their assessment of price stability in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro. It does not apply to non-euro-area countries.
EU countries must provide the Commission with a flash estimate of the HICP every 2 years.
The Commission must adopt implementing acts specifying the breakdown of the flash estimate by currency and the sub-index weights for the different indices.
Each year, EU countries must update the index weights for each of the indices and provide a report to the Commission on the quality of the data provided.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING ACT APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Commission uses the harmonised index to monitor price stability.
For more information, see:
The Harmonised indices (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMINVENTION
Council Regulation (EC) No 596/2014/EU — Harmonised price index
REFERNomenclature of the European Consumer Price Index (HCPI).
KEY TERMSUMINARY
The harmonised consumer price index is a composite index based on a number of categories of goods, such as foodstuffs, clothing and footwear. It is not intended to be a cost-of-living index.
It does not take into account the effect of tax rates on products.
In addition, the index does not include the prices charged by social security systems, social security schemes and social assistance schemes.
This regulation does not affect the calculation of the euro’s value added tax rate (VAT) or the value of social security contributions to the general budget of EU countries, which are subject to the same tax rates as national VAT rates.
HARMONISED INDICATIONS
The legislation sets out the rules for harmonising the indices. These rules apply from 1 January 2021.
These rules apply to the following categories of products:
foodstuffs;
medicinal products;
personal protective equipment;
goods and services;
services;
housing and utilities;
telecommunications and internet access;
other services; and
services.
National authorities must ensure that:
the data provided by EU countries are accurate and comparable;
there is no distortion of competition between EU countries’ national indices;
the quality of data provided is comparable and comparable to those provided by other EU countries;
data provided by the Commission are reliable and up-to-date;
information provided by national authorities is comparable to the national indices provided by Eurostat and Eurostat;
national authorities are responsible for ensuring that the data they provide comply with the requirements laid down in the legislation.
WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYING?
From 1 January 2020. EU countries had to transmit the data for the period 2016 to 2016 by 31 December 2018.
From 31 December 2019, the Commission had to submit to the European Parliament and the Council a report on the suitability of the OOH price index to integrate into the HicP coverage. This report must be made available to the Council and the Commission by 31 March 2020. The Commission must also present a report in the light of the fact that the Commission has adopted implementing acts on the compatibility of these two indices with the other EU statistics.
Amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/856
From 30 June 2018, the regulation was amended to take account of the introduction of the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) price index. This means that the OECD price index will now be included in the index. The OHD price index, which is based on the average price of food and services in the euro area, will be integrated into the index starting from 31 December 2020 and will be used for the first time in the second half of 2021. This amendment will allow the OHD index to be used as a reference for the euro-area indices. The new OOH index will replace the previous ODI price index which had to be replaced by the ODI in the first half of 2020.
Repeal
From 2 January 2020, the regulations will be repealed and replaced by Regulation (Euratom) 2020/854.
Regulations (EC, EFTA, EC, EEC, EEA) No 12



Summary 23:
EU anti-dumping duties — rules on imports and exports of certain goods from non-EU countries (until 31 December 2020)
EU Anti-Dumping duties— rules on import and export of certain products (until 1 January 2021)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — rules for the application of anti-Doping duties
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the application by the European Commission of the EU’s rules on trade in goods from outside the EU.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 596/2004.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to imports of goods originating in a market-economy third country (non-EU country).
It does not apply to imports originating in an EU country.
Importers must register with the Commission if they believe that the goods they import are subject to unfair competition.
The Commission may impose provisional or definitive duties on imports from a third country if it considers that the imports are likely to harm the EU industry.
EU industry may also request that the import be subject to registration if it believes that:
the goods are being imported illegally;
the importation is likely to adversely affect EU industry;
there is a risk that the imported goods will not be used in the EU market;
it is likely that the products will be used for purposes other than those for which they are intended.
If the Commission considers that these goods are not being used for the purposes for which are intended, it may impose protective measures.
Where protective measures are necessary, the Commission may decide to terminate the investigation or proceedings.
Procedure
The investigation may be initiated by the Commission. The Commission must inform the parties to the investigation and proceedings and give them 10 days to comment on the information presented by the other interested parties.
In the event that the Commission decides to terminate an investigation, it must publish a notice in the Official Journal of the European Union announcing the reasons for its decision.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to protect EU industry against unfair competition, including:
measures to protect consumers from the adverse effects of unfair competition;
measures designed to ensure fair competition in the market.
For more information, see:
Trade in goods (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Imports: imports of products originating in another EU country which are not subject to duties.
* Exports: goods imported by a third party from a country which is not a market economy.
ACT
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/722 of 29 November 2018 on the codification of Regulation (EEC) no 596 / 2004 on the protection of consumers from unfair competition (OJ L 183, 31.12.2018, pp. 1-2)
RELATED ACTS
Regulation (EC, Eur) No 1082/2009 of the EC, Euramax of 29 June 2009 on the prevention of unfair trade in products originating outside the European Economic Area (Euramax) (Codified version).

Summary 24:
Investigations and proceedings in the field of customs clearance — EU rules on investigations and proceedings against undertakings for alleged breaches of customs customs duties
InvestIGATIONS AND PROCEDURES
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — investigations and investigations into breaches of Customs clearance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for investigating and prosecuting suspected infringements of Customs Customs clearance rules.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 552/98 which had previously been in force.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all goods and services imported into the EU from non-EU countries. It does not apply to goods imported into EU countries that are not subject to customs clearance.
EU countries are responsible for investigating, prosecuting and imposing definitive duties on the importation of goods or services.
Implementing duties
The European Commission may impose definitive duties if it considers that:
the importation, export or transit of goods is likely to cause serious damage to the EU’s economic, social or cultural interests;
there is a risk that the import of goods may be contrary to public policy, public security or public health;
the goods are likely to be used for purposes other than those for which they are intended;
it is likely that the goods are intended to cause harm to the interests of the EU as a whole or part of a group of people.
The Commission may decide to impose a definitive duty on goods imported by a third party, such as:
for example, if they are used for the manufacture, transport, manufacture, importation or transport of goods intended for a private domestic use;
if the goods were intended for the purpose of a commercial service, for example, or
for the purposes of a service, service or service for which is not intended for public consumption;
in the event of a breach of the customs clearance rules, if the goods concerned are intended for use for commercial purposes.
Procedures
The investigation and proceedings are carried out by the Commission. They may include:
investigations into allegations of breaches of duties;
prosecutions against persons, companies or bodies suspected of breaching customs clearance;
exhaustive and proportionate investigations into the alleged breach of duties.
Where an EU country considers that an investigation or proceedings are necessary, it may impose protective measures, including:
restrictive measures;
suspension or withdrawal of the undertaking;
imposition of definitive duties; or
imposing a ban on imports of goods.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2016/856 entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Importers’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Customs clearance: the process by which goods are imported into or exported from the EU by means of a customs clearance procedure.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Delegated regulation (EU), Euratom, of 29 June 2015, on the investigation and investigation procedures relating to breaches of the Customs clearance Rules (Codified version) (OJ L 183, 30.6.2015, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  (1) The European Parliament and of the Council adopt Regulation (EC) 2017/853 of 29 May 2017 on the application of the European Union Customs Code to the internal market and repealing Regulations (EC, EEC, EC, EC) No 652/96 and (EC), No 553/98, and Council Regulation (ES) No 853/96 (EC).
See consolidated version.

Summary 27:
Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 on the carriage of passengers on the railway
Regulation(EU) 2017/1369 on the passenger carriage charge
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/1769 — carriage charge for rail passengers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that passengers travelling by rail on the European Union’s railway network are not disadvantaged by the carriage charge.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (ECB No 1372/2007, which had been amended several times.
KEY POINTS
  
The regulation applies to all passengers travelling on the rail network between the stations of Brussels, Amsterdam, Copenhagen and Amsterdam.
The legislation applies to:
passengers travelling by train, tram, trolleybus, bus, coach, coach or tram;
traffic on trains operated by the national railway undertakings;
people with disabilities and persons with reduced mobility;
persons travelling by coach, bus or tram.
Passengers are entitled to receive a response within a reasonable period of time from the railway undertaking or the station manager to their request for a carriage charge and to be informed of the reasons for the extension of the service or the replacement service.
Where a passenger is refused access to a carriage, the passenger is entitled to compensation. The carriage charge may include the payment of compensation to the person in question.
A passenger may be refused entry to the railway station if:
the carriage is operated by a different railway undertaking;
the passenger is not accompanied by a person with a disability;
a passenger is accompanied by someone with a reduced mobility.
If a passenger does not meet the above one of the above conditions, the person concerned may be entitled to a refund of the cost of the ticket.
In addition, the regulation provides for a number of other measures to protect the rights of persons with disabilities, including:
access to a free baggage registration voucher;
refusal to board a train if there is no space available for bicycles in the carriage space;
information to persons who are disabled or who have reduced mobility and who are unable to use a wheelchair.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive applies from 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
Passenger carriage charge (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Carriage charge: a charge charged to passengers for the right to travel by rail between a railway station and a point of departure.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1769/2007 of 21 December 2007 on the application of the carriage-charge regulation for railway passengers.

Summary 30:
Reporting of breaches of EU law that are harmful to the public interest: protection of ‘whistleblowers’ (Directive 2014/60/EU)
Reporting breaches of Union law: protecting the rights of whistleblowers (OJ L 183, 29.10.2014, pp. 1-8)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2014/62/EU — protection of whistleblowers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the protection of persons who are victims of serious breaches of European Union law (EU law) and to protect them against retaliation.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that persons who have been the subject of a breach of the EU law are protected against retaliation when they report such breaches.
The directive applies to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality.
It does not apply to EU citizens who are a national of a non-EU country or nationals of an EU country.
National security is the sole responsibility of each EU country, but the directive also applies to persons who work for, or live in, or have worked for, the European Union (EU).
The protection of reporting persons is based on a number of factors, including:
the nature of the breach;
the seriousness of the offence; and
the level of protection provided for by national law.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?

Summary 31:
European Integrated Border Management Agency
European integrated border management Agency
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — European integrated border control
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets up the European Integrated Border Control Agency (EIBM).
KEY POINTS
The EIBM is the EU’s border control agency. It contributes to the detection, prevention and combating of cross- challenges and threats at the external borders of EU countries.
It is responsible for:
promoting and supporting the implementation of European integrated borders management in all EU countries;
contributing to the prevention, detection, control and combating cross-border crime;
providing support to EU countries’ border management support teams;
monitoring and assessing the effectiveness of the EU border control system;
assisting EU countries in their implementation of EU border management policies.
The regulation lays down a number of specific tasks, including:
establishing a standing corps of border control officers;
coordinating the deployment of liaison officers at the EU borders;
preparing and evaluating the performance of the standing corps;
exchanging information and best practices;
developing and implementing a common approach to border control;
ensuring the coordination of border controls;
supporting EU countries and the European Commission in their border control systems;
reviewing the effectiveness and implementation of the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that:
the standing corps is based on the best available information;
the number of officers deployed at their borders is not less than 1,000;
1,000 liaison officers are deployed per EU country;
there are at least 1,500 liaison officers in each EU country.
Each EU country must designate a national border control authority responsible for managing its own borders.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a series of measures to improve border control. These include:
implementing the European Border and Coast Guard Code of Conduct (EBCC);
introducing the European Arrest Warrant System (EASO);
creating a European Asylum and Migration Information System (EMISA);
impleasing EU countries to cooperate more closely with the EASO and Europol.
For more information, see:
EU border control (European Commission).

Summary 32:
European Network of Information and Communications Technology Security Assurance Agency (ENISA)
European Networking and Certification Agency (ENC)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — setting up a European Networking & Certification Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish a European network of information and communication technology (ICT) cybersecurity certification authorities (ENISA).
It sets up a network of national cybersecurity certification bodies (NCCBs) to ensure that cybersecurity certification schemes and certificates are compatible across the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The aim of ENISA is to:
ensure that the European cybersecurity certification scheme is compatible with the EU’s national cybersecurity legislation;
provide a common framework for the certification of ICT products, ICT service and ICT process certificates;
promote interoperability and interoperability between national cybersecurity certificates and national cybersecurity schemes;
contribute to the development of a single EU cybersecurity certification system.
The regulation sets out a number of objectives for ENISA, including:
encouraging the development and use of cybersecurity-related products, services and processes;
ensuring a high level of cybersecurity awareness and awareness of the risks associated with cybersecurity;
developing a common approach to cybersecurity awareness-raising and education;
supporting EU countries’ efforts to improve cybersecurity awareness, education, training and training;
strengthening cybersecurity awareness among the public and the private sector;
cooperating with third countries and international organisations;
exchanges of experience and best practice;
facilitating the exchange of best practices and best practices;
raising awareness of cybersecurity issues;
helping the private and public sector to develop cybersecurity skills;
creating an EU-wide network of cybersecurity experts;
establishing a European Cybersecurity Information Society (ECIS) and a European Information Society Information Society Society (ECAIS);
promoting cooperation between EU countries, EU countries and non-EU countries in the field of cybersecurity.
ENISA is managed by a management board composed of representatives of the EU and EU countries. It is chaired by an Executive Director appointed by the Commission and is assisted by a Management Board.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLYORK INTO FORCE?

Summary 33:
European Union’s own resources — statistics on gross national income (GNI)
European Union's own resources – statistics on Gross National Income
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 608/2014 laying down implementing measures for the system of own resources of the European Union (OJ L 168, 7.6.2014)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the collection, transmission and use of gross national incomes (GNIs) by EU countries for EU own resources purposes.
It aims to ensure that GNI data are reliable, exhaustive and comparable.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to GNI statistics collected by the European Commission (Eurostat) and the European Statistical System (ESS).
EU countries must submit to Eurostat the following data:
gross national income* for each EU country;
the sources and methods used to compile these data;
a list of sources of GNI information;
an inventory of the data; and
a report on the methods used and the results of the calculations.
Eurostat must ensure that the data are accurate, comparable and exhaustive.
The European Commission is responsible for compiling the data and for ensuring that they conform with ESA 2010 and that they do not over-explode.
In addition, EU countries must:
provide Eurostat with all the necessary information, including:
the source and methods for compiling GNI;
data on the sources of income and sources;
information on the GNI categories and methods;
types of income;
methods used to calculate GNIneffective GNI calculations;
statistical confidentiality rules on statistical confidentiality;
assess the reliability of the methods and data used;
check that the calculations are comparable and that the methods are exhaustive;
keep a record of all the data they have provided;
report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of their GNI calculation methods.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The GNI is the most important indicator of economic activity in the EU and is used by the Commission to assess EU countries’ economic and social policies.
For more information, see:
‘Gross national income’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Gross national income: the sum of all income and wealth generated by an economy in a given year.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of vehicles and light commercial vehicles
EU type–approval
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — type–applicability of certain types of vehicles to the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that vehicles designed and built in the EU have the same legal status as their counterparts in the United Kingdom (UK) when they are imported into the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles, including buses, coaches and trucks;
motorcycles;
trams;
cars;
light commercial vehicles;
bicycles;
horses;
personal protective equipment (PPE) vehicles.
It does not apply to certain categories of vehicles, such as motorcycles, quadricycles, light commercial tractors and light recreational vehicles (LCEVs).
It applies to vehicles designed or built for use on the roads of the EU and to vehicles intended for use in non-EU countries.
Manufacturers must apply to the EU type‑approval authority for the same type of vehicle to which they have previously applied in the UK.
They must submit a request for a EU type‐approval to the competent authority.
The request may include any EC type‐approved vehicle, EU type–approved vehicle or UN type‐applicable vehicle and its attachments, as part of the whole vehicle.
Application for a Union type-approved vehicle
The competent authority may refuse to grant a UK type‑approved vehicle if:
the vehicle does not meet the requirements of the relevant EU legislation;
it does not comply with the requirements laid down by the EU legislation or with regard to conformity assessment, conformity assessment or conformity assessment;
there is no clear distinction between EU and non‑EU-approved vehicles; or if the vehicle has not been certified by the competent authorities of the Member States;
the type-applicant has not carried out a conformity assessment in accordance with the applicable EU legislation.
If a manufacturer fails to comply with these requirements, the vehicle is considered to be a non‐compliant vehicle and may be subject to EU type-conditions.
A manufacturer may apply for a European type-authorisation for a vehicle which has not yet been certified in the relevant Member State and which is intended to be tested in the Member State in which it is built.
When a manufacturer does so, the manufacturer is responsible for the obligations stemming from the UK type-clearer’s approval. The manufacturer is not liable for any acts or omissions of the UK body.
Where a manufacturer submits a request to a competent authority for a specific type, the applicant must provide the relevant information, including the vehicle type and the type of the vehicle, as well as any relevant EU or non‐EU conformity assessment and conformity assessment results.
In the case of vehicles that have already been certified, the request must include the relevant EC type–certified vehicle and the relevant UN type–conformity assessment results and any other relevant information.
Applications for a type‐certificate of conformity assessment must be submitted by the manufacturer and, where applicable, accompanied by a copy of the type–clearing certificate issued by the European Commission or the competent national conformity assessment authority. If the manufacturer does not provide the necessary information, the type‐clearer may refuse the application.
Type–clearer legislation may be amended by means of delegated acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Type–clearence certificate: a certificate that indicates that a vehicle meets the relevant requirements of EU legislation and that it complies with the relevant conformity assessment rules.
* EU–cleared vehicle: a vehicle designed or manufactured in the European Economic Area (EEA) and approved by the Commission and certified in compliance with EU legislation, and which meets the requirements set out in Directive 2007/46/EC, Regulation (EC) No 167/2013, Regulation(EU) No 168/2013 and Regulation (EU), and which has been amended several times.

Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules on the protection of personal data and the processing of EU classified information
Europol — rules protecting personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2013/488/EU establishing a European Police Office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes rules to protect personal data in the European Union (EU) against the threat of crime and terrorism.
It aims to ensure that personal data are protected when they are processed by Europol.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the rules for the protection and processing of personal and non-personal data.
The data protection officer (EDPS) is responsible for protecting the personal data of EU citizens who are the subject of a criminal investigation or who have been a victim of an act of violence or who are suspected of committing a criminal offence. The EDPS must:
ensure that the data are processed in accordance with the law of the EU country in which they are stored;
notify the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the data they have processed and the reasons for their processing;
act as a single point of contact for all EU countries and EU countries’ competent authorities;
use the same procedures for processing personal data as those used by the national authorities of EU countries or EU countries;
keep a record of all personal data that it has processed;
provide a statistical overview of all data that has been processed.
Data protection
The EDPS has the power to:
inform the European Data Protection Officer and the national competent authorities of the processing operations;
assess whether personal data have been processed in a way that is necessary and proportionate to prevent, detect or investigate serious crimes;
take decisions on the storage, processing and use of data;
make a decision on the continued storage of data, and on the deletion of data. If no decision is taken on storage, that data will be erased automatically after 3 years.
EU countries must ensure that the EDPS does not:
exchange personal data with non-EU countries or the United Kingdom (1) unless it is necessary to prevent or investigate a serious crime;
process personal data for the purpose of preventing, detecting or investigating serious crimes.
In addition, personal data may only be processed for the purposes of preventing or investigating criminal offences.
Personal data may not be shared between EU countries, except in exceptional cases where it is essential for the prevention, detection or investigation of serious crimes or for the investigation and prosecution of a serious offence. However, the data may be exchanged between the EU and its Member States. The security of such exchanges must be ensured.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
This decision has applied since 1 May 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions adopted the Europol Convention in 2009.
For more information, see:
‘Europol’ on the European External Action Service’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2013/489/EU of 21 March 2013 establishing a framework for the establishment of a European police office and repealing Council Decision 2009/371/JHA (OJ L 183, 29.4.2013, pp. 1-8)

EU financial support for the CoVID-2019 outbreak and the impact on social security systems

Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 — EU financial assistance to the EU countries affected by the CO VID-19 epidemic
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It enables the European Union (EU) to provide financial assistance in the form of loans to EU countries that are in difficulties or seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond its control.
It aims to help EU countries to cope with the impact of COVID‐19, the outbreak of the severe acute respiratory syndrome (SARS) in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
EU countries may borrow from the EU budget to cover their own resources.
The borrowing and lending operations under the instrument are carried out by the European Commission and the European Central Bank (ECB).
Borrowing and lending
The Commission may borrow up to €500 million from its own resources and €250 million from the ECB.
Contributions to the Instrument are made in form of guarantees from EU countries. These guarantees are irrevocable, unconditional, unconditional and on-demand guarantees that are guaranteed by the EU country concerned.
the Commission must conclude an agreement with a contributing EU country on the terms and conditions of the guarantee before the loan is made available.
In addition, the Commission may provide guarantees on behalf of the EU on the basis of an agreement concluded between the EU and the contributing country.

It has applied since 30 March 2020.

measures to prevent and control the spread of the disease;
measures for the prevention and control of its spread;
supporting the social partners in the fight against the disease.
