Summary 37:
Resolution of insolvent financial institutions — common rules for equity holders and creditors
Resolving insolvency
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 — common principles for resolution of insolvencies of financial institutions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that equity holders of a failing financial institution (i.e. a financial institution which is insolvent) and their creditors are not disadvantaged in the event of its resolution.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down common principles and procedures for the resolution of failing financial institutions (CCPs).
It applies to all financial institutions, including banks, insurance companies and pension funds.
It does not apply to banks and insurance companies.
EU countries must ensure that:
CCPs are able to draw up and implement a recovery plan for their equity holders;
the creditor losses are not greater than the losses that they would have incurred under normal insolvent proceedings;
securities and other assets of a CCP are not subject to liquidation proceedings.
In addition, the regulation sets out rules for the use of the position allocation and loss allocation tools (PAILs), which are designed to allow equity holders to absorb losses first in resolution in a way that minimises the risk of legal challenge by equity holders, on the basis that their losses in resolution are greater than their losses that would have suffered under normal bankruptcy proceedings according to the ‘no creditor worse off’ principle.
A resolution plan must be drawn up by the competent authorities of the CCP and its parent undertaking. It must also include:
a recovery plan;
a valuation of the equity holders’s assets and liabilities;
an objective and scope of the loss allocation and position allocation tools;
information to be provided by the creditor to equity holders on the structure and structure of the recovery plan.
The creditor losses must be absorbed by the CCP as soon as possible and not later than 30 days after the end of the bankruptcy proceedings. The creditor losses should not be greater than those of the creditor.
Where the creditor is not a shareholder in a CCP, the creditor should be entitled to a fair share of the proceeds of the resolution proceedings. However, this may not be more than 10% of the total value of the assets.
If the creditor does not benefit from the benefits of a resolution plan, he or she is entitled to the full amount of the capital that the creditor would have received under normal proceedings. If the creditor has not benefited from the benefit of a restructuring plan, they are entitled to full recovery of their losses.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 			
For more information, see:
‘Resolution’ on the European Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force — Date of expiry

Official Journal

-

Summary 39:
The European Social Development Centre (ESDC)
The European social development centre (ESSD)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2013/189/CFSP on the establishment of an ESDC
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes the European Society for the Development of Social Development (ESSDC).
The ESDC is a European Union (EU) non-governmental organisation (NGO) that supports research and development (R & D) in the field of social sciences.
It is funded by the general budget of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Funding
Each year, the ESDC receives an annual or multi-annual contribution from the EU general budget. The ESDC can apply for research and other projects in the area of CFSP. The general budget covers the costs of:
research and development;
programmes;
operational activities;
education and training;
exchange of information and best practice;
research, development and dissemination of research results;
development of e-learning content;
training of researchers;
support for the development of research and training programmes;
organisation of conferences, symposia and workshops;
reciprocal access to research facilities;
facilities for the dissemination of information, training and dissemination;
contributions to research projects;
funding to the European Union Institute for Strategic Studies (EUISS);
exchanges of information on social sciences and related to CFSP;
financial support for research, development, training, dissemination and dissemination.
Budget
the ESDC’s budget is based on a balanced between the EU and the general EU budget.
The general budget is divided into 2 parts:
1.General budget
The General budget comprises revenue and expenditure of €1.5 billion for the current financial year (2013-2016) and €2 billion for its subsequent financial year.
2.Programmes
The EUISS is the main source of financial support for ESDC. The EUISS provides the European Research and Innovation Centre (ERIC) with:
expertise;
information and training on the European social sciences;
technical support;
instructions on the development and use of eLearning materials;
access to research;
visits and exchanges of researchers, researchers and researchers’ staff;
participation in conferences, seminars, workshops and conferences;
assistance in the development, management and implementation of research projects.
In addition, the EUISS may provide lectures given by EUISS analysts and contribute to the further development of ESDC e-Learning content.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?

Summary 41:
EU blue card and residence permits for non-EU nationals working illegally in another EU country
European Union blue card
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/33/EU on the application of the EU blue card to non-European Union nationals who are illegally working in the territory of an EU country other than their own
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU blue cards issued by EU countries to nationals of non-euro area countries working illegally within the EU are respected and enforced.
It is part of a package of measures designed to improve the integration of migrants into the labour market.
KEY POINTS
EU blue cards
The blue card is issued by an employer to a non-Euro-area national who is illegally working for an employer in another European Union (EU) country. It is valid for a period of 5 years and valid for an additional period of 3 years.
The card is valid in the EU country where the employer is resident.
EU countries must ensure that the employer and the employer’s contact points provide each other with all relevant information, including:
the name and address of the employer;
the date and place of birth of the employee;
any other relevant information;
a description of the nature and nature of the job;
details of the activities of the business or organisation where the employee is employed;
where the employee has been convicted of a criminal offence;
information on the nature, nature and duration of the employment contract;
how the employee was fired or dismissed.
Employers must provide the contact points with all the relevant information.
the contact points must also provide information on:
business activities that an EU Blue Card holder may engage in in the country concerned;
business activity in which an EU blue Card holder from another Member State is allowed to engage; and
the procedures applicable to obtaining an EU visa, residence permits and family members in a second EU country.
In addition, EU countries must:
provide information on the conditions under which the employer or the contact point may refuse to issue a blue card or residence permit to an EU citizen;
ensure that employers and contact points cooperate to ensure the protection of the rights and interests of employees and their family members.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive has applied since 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a comprehensive labour migration policy and for better integration.
For more information, see:
‘Migration and integration’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2014/34/EU of 20 December 2014 on the establishment of a European Union Blue Card (OJ L 183, 31.12.2014, pp. 1-8)

EU migration management programme — financial support for EU countries’s external borders and migration management programmes (2018-2021)
EU immigration management programme: financial support to EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2018/2021 — EU funding for migration management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It aims to support EU countries in their efforts to control their borders and reduce irregular arrivals and the number of deaths at sea.

BACKGROUND

preventing illegal immigration;
refugees and their return to their country of origin;
training activities;
assistance for asylum seekers;

cooperation between EU countries and non-EU countries.

Facilitation of access to work and leave
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/33/EC — flexible working conditions for women and men
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the protection of female and male workers who are pregnant or have a child under the age of 18 and who are taking maternity or paternity leave.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all EU workers, irrespective of their gender.
It does not apply to:
carers’ leave;
part-time workers;
carer’s leave; or
care workers who have a partner.
EU countries may specify the modalities of application of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 January 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
European Union (EU) countries must ensure that workers who take maternity leave and/or take paternity leave are protected from dismissal and any preparatory steps for a possible dismissal on the grounds that they have applied for, or have taken, such leave or have exercised their right to request such leave. The directive is part of a package of measures designed to ensure equal treatment for women, men and children.

Summary 44:
Fraud affecting the EU’s financial interests: minimum rules for criminal offences and sanctions
Fraud involving the EU's financial interests; minimum rules to protect the European Communities’ financial interests
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/829 on the protection of the European Union’ s financial interests against fraud affecting the European Community’’
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that criminal offences* and sanctions* are applied to fraud* and corruption* that could affect the EU budget.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
procurement fraud;
tax evasion;
abuse of public procurement contracts;
any other form of fraud that could harm the EU financial interests.
It does not apply to tax evasion.
EU countries may in principle continue to apply administrative measures and penalties in parallel in the area covered by this directive.
Sanctions
The legislation provides for a maximum penalty of at least 4 years’ imprisonment for the most serious offences and at least 10 years for the more serious offences. The maximum level of maximum imprisonment is set out in the annex to the directive. In addition, EU countries may impose fines of up to €500,000 for each of the above-mentioned offences.
Penalties may also be imposed on natural persons and on companies.
The European Commission and the competent national authorities must take into account the requirements of confidentiality and the rules on data protection.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 July 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of anti-fraud measures to protect its financial interests in recent years. These include:
the EU Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF);
the European Anti-Corruption Office (EACO);
measures to combat fraud against the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF) (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘Fraud and corruption’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Procurement Fraud: any act or omission by a public authority to obtain, use or dispose of goods or services in a way that is contrary to the public interest.
ACT
Council Directive (EU, Euratom) 2017 /829 of 5 July 2017 on the Protection of the Union's Financial Interest Against Fraud (OJ L 183, 7.7.2017, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
 
Directives (EUEC, EFTA, ECB, European Court of Justice, European Parliament and Council of Justice) 2016/852 of 29 June 2016 on the prevention and investigation of fraud and corruption and repealing Council Directive (EC) No 596/2004 of 29 May 2004 on the Prevention and Investigation of Bribery and Corruption and Council Directive 2004/7/EC of 29 April 2004 on preventing and investigating corruption and corruption.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?

The directive applies to debit cards and credit cards. It does not apply to credit cards, which are not covered by the directive.

Summary 47:
Data collection and transmission of statistical data on imports and exports of goods and services from non-EU countries
Data gathering and sharing of statistical information on import and export statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2016/1954 — statistical data collection and dissemination
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1365/2006, which had been amended several times.
It aims to improve the quality of the statistical data collected by the European Commission (Eurostat).
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the procedures for collecting and transmitting statistical data to Eurostat, the European Union’s statistical office.
The data are collected by means of the NUTS codes (National Transport Safety System codes) and the NIST codes (NUTS 2 and NIST 3 codes).
Data collection
Eurostat must collect and transmit the data to the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC), which is composed of representatives of EU countries and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC).
The Commission must adopt implementing acts laying down the methodological requirements and criteria designed to ensure that the data produced are of a high standard. These include data interchange standards. The Commission must also assess the quality and reliability of the data transmitted.
EU countries’ statistical offices must ensure that:
the data transmitted comply with the statistical requirements laid down in the regulation;
the statistical procedures laid down by Eurostat are followed;
there is a cost-effectiveness analysis, including an assessment of the burden on respondents and of the production costs of producing the data;
data quality is ensured.
Data transmitted must be sent in a language easily understood by the EU country of registration of the vessel.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The delegated regulation has applied since 8 December 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* NUSTS codes: the codes used to describe the national codes of vessels registered in different EU countries.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 50:
Eurojust — rules for the European External Action Service (Eurojust) — rules on operational data protection and cooperation
Eurojust – rules on operations
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) No 1271/2013 — Eurojust
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for Eurojust, the European Union institution, body, office or agency responsible for the protection of operational personal data and cooperation with other EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down Eurojust’s rules on:
operational data protection;
cooperation with the European Parliament, the Council, the Commission and the European Police and Criminal Justice Office (PPO);
exchanges of information with the EU countries and between national members;
exchange of operational data with the EPPO and other EU bodies, bodies and agencies;
transmission of data to the European Commission.
The European Parliament and the Council are responsible for ensuring that Eurojust complies with the relevant EU rules on data protection.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
Decision 2013/13/EU sets up Eurojust.
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the first and only EU institution to be established under the Lisbon Treaty.
For more information, see:
About Eurojust (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1399/2013/2014/JHA of 21 December 2013 laying down the rules on the functioning of Eurojust and repealing Delegislating Regulation (JHA) No 596/2009 (see summary).
KEY TERMS
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1482/2013
1.1.2014
-
Obligations of EU institutions and bodies and bodies
The Eurojust is responsible for:
protecting operational data;
ensuring that operational data are protected;
providing the Commission with the necessary information to carry out its tasks;
assisting the Commission in its work;
contributing to the prevention, investigation and prosecution of crime;
supporting the fight against corruption;
helping the Commission to implement its work.
Role of the European Political and Security Committee (EPPO)
The EPPO plays a key role in Eurojust's work. It assists the Commission, the EU institutions or bodies, including the Commission's Directorate-General for Justice and Security, in the implementation of the EU's internal market surveillance policy.
In addition, the EPPO assists Eurojust in:
monitoring and analysing the impact of EU legislation on the EU budget;
advising the Commission on the application of EU law;
sharing information on EU legislation;
developing and sharing best practice;
preparing reports on EU policies, programmes and programmes;
coordinating with the Commission;
carrying out research and development activities;
participating in the activities of other EU organisations and bodies, such as the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF), the European Court of Auditors, the Court of Justice of Europe or the European Statistical System Committee.
Eligibility for EU funding
The Commission may grant EU funding to Eurojust under certain conditions, including if it is necessary for the legitimate performance of tasks covered by the competence of the other EU institution or body, body or agency. The Commission must inform the Commission of these conditions in writing.
Financial rules
The Regulation lays down the financial rules applicable to the Executive Board. The Executive Board is composed of representatives of EU countries. It is assisted by an Administrative Director and a College of Experts. The College adopts the annual programming document, which is updated annually.
Each year, Eurojust adopts a budget for the period 2014-2020. The budget must be approved by the executive board and the Commission. It must also adopt the programming document and the annual work programme, which must be adopted by the College.
EU funding may be frozen if the Commission considers that the budget does not meet the needs of the Commission or the EU institution, bodies or bodies concerned.
Amendments to the regulation
In 2018, the regulation was amended by Decision (EU/2018/EU) 2018/852 to take account of the fact that the Commission is no longer a member of the Eurojust Executive Board and that it has been replaced by the European Judicial Network (EJN). This means that the European Parliamentary and Council Committee on the Protection of Civil Liberties and Fundamental Freedoms is now responsible for implementing the regulation.
This amendment was adopted by Council Decision 2013/1482/EU on behalf of the Council.
Decisions 2013/15/EU and 2013/16/EU have been amended several times, most recently in 2018.
Last updated: 28.08.2014



Summary 51:
Fighting money laundering by means of criminal law (Directive 2015/849/JHA) (European Union).
Fighting money trafficking by means OF CRIME
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/884 — combating money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat money laundering through criminal law, enabling more efficient and swifter cross-border cooperation between EU countries’ authorities.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that money laundering is criminalised in their national law.
The directive does not apply to money laundering committed by negligence.
It does not cover:
money laundering committed for profit;
laundering of goods, services or property;
the laundering of services, goods or property by a third party.
Penalties for money laundering are based on a number of factors, including:
the nature and seriousness of the offence;
where the offence is committed in another EU country;
whether the offence was committed by a natural person or by a non-EU national.
Prosecutions must not be impeded by the fact that the criminal activity was committed in an EU country or in a third country, subject to the conditions set out in this directive.
National authorities must cooperate with each other and with the European Anti-Fraud Office (Eurojust).
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council assessing the added value of this directive by 3 December 2023.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
It entered into force on 3 December 2015.
.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:

* KEY TERMS
* Money laundering: the laundering of funds, services, property, goods, goods and services by third parties.

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the EU’s fishing plan for bluefin tuna
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules to ensure that EU countries’ purse seiners comply with the rules of the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
purse seining vessels are equipped to fish for tuna and have an individual quota;
each purse seiner is equipped to catch tuna over 24 metres in length;
there is a limit on the number of vessels that can be used per year;
the annual quota is set by the EU country concerned.
The regulation does not apply to vessels that are not equipped for tuna fishing.
It does not cover the fishing of tuna caught by non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Norway, Switzerland and the United States of America.

Summary 53:
Injunctive pricing: combating injurious pricing in the European Union (EU)
Insulting pricing: preventing unfair pricing in EU industry
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — combating injurer pricing in European Union industry
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to protect consumers from unfair pricing* in the EU’s internal market.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 182/2011.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU-regulated goods and services. It does not apply to goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1), Ireland or the United States of America (2), or goods imported from China (3).
It does not cover:
the sale of goods to third countries;
the purchase of goods by a buyer from outside the EU; or
the transfer of goods between EU countries.
Scope
It applies to:
all EU-registered companies, except for certain small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) which are subject to EU competition law;
all companies which have their registered office in an EU country other than the EU country in which they are based;
any company which has its registered office;
companies which are owned by a foreign national.
Injunctions
Where an EU company is involved in a dispute, it must inform the European Commission of its intention to initiate proceedings against the EU company.
The Commission must:
inform the Commission of the reasons for initiating proceedings;provide all relevant evidence and of the remedies available to it;make available to the Commission within 10 working days of receiving the complaint.
If the Commission considers that a complaint has been filed, the Commission must decide whether to launch proceedings. If the Commission decides that the complaint has not been filed within 10 days, the proceedings may be terminated.
Where a complaint is withdrawn, the case may be referred to the Court of Justice of the EU or to the European Court of Auditors.
Disclosure of information
EU companies must provide the Commission with all relevant information to enable it to make a decision on whether to start proceedings. This information must be provided in writing.
EU countries must ensure that the information provided is accurate, complete and not misleading.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
Injurious pricing (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Injurer pricing: when a price is higher than the cost price of the goods or services for which the price is calculated.
* Irregular pricing: a price which is not justified by economic reality and which is unjustified because of market conditions.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 652/2011 of 20 December 2011 on injurious prices in the internal market (Codified version)
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Directive 2011/24/EU
1.1.2019
-

Summary 54:
Cableway installations — safety and environmental aspects
Cabling and safety aspects
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2000/9/EC — EU rules on cableway installations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It lays down rules for the installation and operation of cableways (i.e. the cableway system) in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that cableways are safe for users and the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to installations located within the territory of the EU.
It does not apply to cableways which are located outside the EU, but which are intended to be used for the transport of goods, passengers or other persons by road, rail, sea, air, inland waterway or other means of transport.
EU countries must lay down procedures for authorising the construction and the entry into service of such installations, as well as for their operation.
The legislation does not cover the installation of cableway systems that are located in non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland, Norway and Switzerland.
Importers must ensure that the installations comply with the relevant EU safety and health requirements.
Manufacturers and importers must:
ensure that the installation complies with the requirements laid down in Annex I to the directive;
provide the relevant information on the installation to the competent national authority;
make sure that the safety components and subsystems or safety components are installed correctly;
have been tested and certified by the competent authorities;
inform the competent authority of the country in which the installation is located;
importers and distributors of the relevant components and the relevant subsystems and safety components before placing them on the market;
notify EU countries of any serious infringements of the legislation;
keep a register of installations that do not conform to the legislation.
Each EU country must designate a national authority responsible for the notification of installations which do not comply with this directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision (EU) 2017/852 amends and repeals Directive 2000/8/EC.
BACKGROUND
The cableway legislation is based on the ‘new approach’ principles, as set out in the Council Resolution of 7 May 1985 on a new approach to technical harmonisation and standards.
For more information, see:
Cableways (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cableway system: a system of cables which are used to transport goods, persons or other objects by means of a cableway.
* Cableway installation: an installation which is designed and constructed to provide a means of providing access to the ground, air or sea for a number of purposes, including:
traffic management;
transport of passengers and other persons;
access to the sea;
the movement of goods and persons; and
the transport of cargo.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services in the internal market — EU rules for microenterprises and small businesses (Directive 2014/35/EU on the accessibility of services and products)
Assessment of the accessibility requirements of services
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/34/EU — minimum requirements for accessibility of products, services and goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that products, including transport services, consumer banking services and e-commerce services, are accessible to people with disabilities.
It also aims to improve the availability of accessible products on the EU’s internal market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
products and services provided by small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs);
services provided by consumers and consumers’ organisations;
electronic communications services;
consumer banking services; and
consumer and retail banking services.
EU countries must ensure that:
all products, whether they are intended for sale or to be delivered, are available in a language easily understood by persons with disabilities;
instructions and information on accessibility are provided in an accessible manner;
all electronic communications services are accessible;
information on accessibility is provided in a clear, understandable and intelligible language;
the information provided by the service provider is clear and understandable;
distributors and economic operators are responsible for ensuring that the information they provide is accessible.
The legislation does not apply to the following services:
telecommunications services, such as the internet, e-mail, the telephone, the internet banking services, the postal service, and the internet café;
banking services, including the payment services and the retail banking service;
telematics;
internet hosting services, i.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that all electronic communication services provided to the public are accessible by means of a user-friendly user interface;
provide the public with all the necessary information to enable them to use the services provided;
make it possible for the public to make use of the services offered;
require that all the information provided in the electronic communications service provided by service providers is accessible, including:
the name and address of the provider;
a description of the physical environment in which the services are provided, including in the form of a printed document;
an indication of the content of the service provided, and of the information to be provided, on the user interface and on the contents of the product;
identify the economic operators dealing with the products;
establish a single point at which the manufacturer can be contacted;
set out the responsibilities of distributors, importers, retailers, wholesalers and other economic operators.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The regulation applies from 27 June 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 27 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive.
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Accessibility: the ability to use a product or service in a way that does not require the user to be physically or visually impaired.
ACT

Summary 56:
Restrictive measures to combat conflict and illegal exploitation of minerals (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Restrictative measures to curb conflict and illicit exploitation of mineral resources (Regulating (EU, Euratom) 2019 /852
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on the Suppliers’ Responsibility and Due Diligence of Mineral Resources
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect human health and the environment against the adverse effects of conflict and the illegal exploitation and trafficking of minerals.
It is part of the EU’s supply chain due diligence policy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to minerals, potentially present in consumer products, that are mined, processed or processed in the Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC).
It does not apply to minerals produced in the DRC or mined in the Congo Republic (Côte d’Ivoire).
EU countries must ensure that:
responsible producers and importers of minerals comply with the regulation;
responsible smelters and refiners comply with its rules;
the EU and EU countries cooperate and exchange information on responsible producers, importers and distributors;
suppliers and distributors cooperate with each other to ensure that the regulation is applied correctly;
mineral traceability and traceability measures are in place;
good manufacturing practice is in place to ensure traceability of minerals;
it is possible to trace back the origin of minerals from the source to the final destination.
Importers and distributors are responsible for the minerals they process and transport.
The European Commission must draw up a register of responsible producers and distributors. This register must be made publicly available on the internet. It must contain information on the companies involved in the supply chain and on the conditions under which they can be held liable for any infringements of the regulation.
Rules applicable to infringements
If a producer or importer is found to have infringed the regulation, they may be subject to fines of up to €10,000 per infringement. The fine may be increased to €20,000 for each infringement if the infringements are carried out by the same importer or by a third party.
If an importer fails to comply with this regulation, the importer may be liable for a fine of at least €5,000.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle conflict and trafficking in minerals. These include:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 852/2013 on traceability (see summary);
Delegated Regulation (Euratom) 2018/856 on the implementation of Regulation (EU), which aims to improve traceability for traceable minerals and to improve the protection of human health, the environment and the health of animals and plants;
Regulations (EC, EFTA, EU) No 552/2014 and (EC), which aim to ensure compliance with the OECD due diligence guidance on the global supply chain.
For more information, see:
‘Due Diligency’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 57:
EU rules on the control of the free circulation of goods
EU controls on the freedom of movement of goods (recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — control of free circulation and the export of goods in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that goods imported into the European Union (EU) are controlled when they are released for free circulation or exported.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) 782/2002 which had been amended several times.
KEY POINTS
   
The regulation applies to goods imported from outside the EU. It does not apply to:
the importation of goods from non-EU countries;
the transit of goods between EU countries; or
the free circulation* or export* of goods within the EU;
free circulation or the free movement* or the release of goods intended for the internal market.
EU countries must ensure that:
goods are controlled by EU countries’ customs authorities;
they are accompanied by a customs declaration;
there is a risk of fraud and that the goods may be used for purposes other than those for which they were imported.
The declaration must be accompanied by:
a description of the goods;
a list of the economic operators and the place where they are established;
information on the goods and the conditions under which the goods are to be imported;
details of the customs authorities responsible for the place of establishment;
where the goods have been imported, the declaration must include the name and address of the place in which the economic operator and the date and time of arrival and departure.
Imporporporters and importers must provide information on:
where goods are imported into or exported;
how the goods were imported; and
the date and place of arrival.
In addition, EU countries must:
provide information on the persons involved in the importation and export;
ensure that the declaration is accompanied by all the necessary documentation;
make the declaration available to the competent customs authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECAST APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE APPLY?
Since 1 January 2005, the regulation has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s internal market has been subject to a wide range of restrictive measures for a number of years, including controls on free circulation, the export and import of goods, and measures to control the movement of capital.
For more information, see:
‘Free circulation’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 58:
Protecting intellectual property rights in the EU against counterfeiting and counterfeiting — EU customs data protection rules
Protecting the EU’s copyright and trademarks — enforcing the EU customs database
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — combating counterfeiting
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect intellectual property (IP) rights by ensuring that customs authorities in EU countries are able to detect and prevent the counterfeiting of goods.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries are protected from counterfeiting;
customs authorities have access to a central database containing information on the import, export and import of goods that are suspected of infringing IP;
the data collected and processed by customs authorities are accurate and up-to-date; and
the penalties for infringements are effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
The regulation does not apply to goods imported into EU countries that are not covered by this regulation.
Importers and importers must inform customs authorities of the goods they intend to bring into their territory.
Customs authorities must also inform the European Commission of the names and addresses of the persons who may be involved in the manufacture, importation or export of infringing goods. Such persons might include intermediaries, where applicable.
Each EU country must establish an application form by means of implementing acts. The applicant must provide the name and address of the person concerned and the date of birth, date of death, place of birth and place of residence. The application must be submitted within 30 working days of receipt of the application. The competent customs department must notify the applicant of its decision granting or rejecting the application within 30 days of receiving the application, and the applicant must submit a copy of the decision to the European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS).
The applicant must also provide details of:
the goods to which the goods infringe the intellectual property right;
where the goods are intended for sale in another EU country;
a description of the nature and nature of the infringing goods;
how the goods were intended for use in a commercial context;
whether the goods had been imported illegally;
any other information the applicant may have provided.
In addition to the above, EU countries must:
ensure that the competent customs authority has access to the central database for the purpose of identifying and preventing the importation of goods suspected of infringements;
use the information provided by the applicant in order to identify the persons responsible for the infringement;
collect and store the personal data of all persons who have been identified as being involved in counterfeiting.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The delegated regulation entered into force on 19 July 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 19 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat counterfeiting, including:
Regulation (EC) No 45/2001 on the prevention of counterfeiting (see summary);
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 552/2004 and (EC), No 553/2004 on the protection of intellectual property and Regulation (Euratom) 2017/849 on the enforcement of copyright and related rights (See summary).
For more information, see:
Counterfeiting and counterfeit goods (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Imitation of goods: goods which are believed to be counterfeit or which are likely to be counterfeited.
ACT
Commission Implementing Decision 2015/884/EU of 20 July 2015 on combating counterfeit goods and on the collection of personal data on the facilitation of customs data (OJ L 157, 29.7.2015, pp. 1-2)
RELATED ACTS
Regulating (EC)(EU) No 605/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 2013 on the Protection of Intellectual Property Rights (OCT) (Official Journal L 183, 28.6.2013)
Directive 2013/13/EU on the Enforcement of Trade-Related Illicit Goods (Official Gazette) (COM(2013) 605 final, 28 June 2013)

Summary 59:
EU customs programme for cooperation between EU countries’ customs authorities and the EU’s external border control authorities (2011-2020)
EU customs program for cooperation within the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2015/849/EU establishing a customs programme at EU level
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a multiannual EU programme to support cooperation between the customs authorities of EU countries and the European Commission.
It aims to improve cooperation between customs authorities in the field of customs and border control.
KEY POINTS
The programme is designed to support the cooperation of customs authorities with the Commission, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EU).
The aim of the programme is to:
promote customs cooperation between national customs authorities;
support the development of customs cooperation with non-EU countries;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice;
encourage the exchange and exchange of best practice on customs cooperation;
improve the efficiency and effectiveness of customs procedures;
contribute to the prevention and control of cross-border crime.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It is assisted by the European Customs Delegated Committee (ECDCC), which is composed of representatives of the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU‘s external borders are one of its main challenges. Given their extensive mandate, customs authorities are effectively the leading authorities for the control of goods at the EU's external borders.
For more efficient and effective border control, the EU needs to develop cooperation between its customs authorities. This is achieved through the creation of a common customs programme.
This decision establishes a European Union customs programme to promote cooperation between European Union countries and their customs authorities, which is aimed at:
improving customs cooperation in the area of customs control;
developing customs cooperation at the level of the Commission;
promoting customs cooperation among EU countries in the fields of transport, customs administration, customs customs and customs information and communication technologies (ICTs);
encouraging customs cooperation on transport security, customs information technology (ICT) and customs data protection.
In addition, the programme aims to promote the use of the Common Communications Network (CCN) for customs cooperation.
ACT
Commission Decision 2011/884/EU of 21 December 2011 establishing a Customs programme at Union level.

Summary 60:
European Pillar of Social Rights (ESF+)
European Pillar OF Social Rights
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — European Pillar of social rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out rules for the EU’s contribution to the European Pillar OF social rights (EaSI).
KEY POINTS
The EaSI is a multiannual investment programme designed to support the implementation of the EU's 20 key principles of social inclusion.
The Pillar is structured in three categories: equal opportunities and access to the labour market; fair working conditions; and social protection and inclusion. It aims to improve the quality of life of the most deprived people in the European Union (EU).
The European Pillar is based on three pillars:
equal opportunities and equal opportunities;
fair work conditions;
equality of access to social protection;
equal protection.
EU countries must develop their own national investment strategies in support of those reform priorities. The programmes may also use programme-specific indicators.
Eligible actions
The programme is open to all EU countries, including the European Free Trade Association (EFTA), the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD), as well as non-EU countries.
Actions eligible for ESF+ funding include:
investment in the areas covered by the Pillar;
public procurement;
investments in research and innovation;
research and technological development;
employment and social entrepreneurship;
education and training;
social inclusion;
health and long-term care;
housing and social housing;
transport;
access to quality services;
equitable access to employment;
financial support for social housing and social assistance;
other forms of social assistance.
In addition, the programme may also provide financial support for:
social housing and childcare,
health care,
education,
employment,
social protection,
housing, and
education.
Procedures
The Commission must ensure that the programmes are implemented in line with the principles of the Pillar. The Commission must also ensure that:
the programmes are based on objective criteria related to the needs of the least deprived persons;
the measures taken are proportionate to the specific needs of those most deprived persons.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The ESF is part of the European Stability Mechanism (ESM).
For more information, see:
‘European Pillar’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* European Pillar: the Pillar is the cornerstone of European social policy. It is designed to ensure that all EU citizens have equal opportunities, access to quality social services, and equal treatment of workers.
* ESF: the European Social Fund is the main instrument for supporting social inclusion in the EU.

Summary 62:
Equal Opportunities Fund (EGF)
Equitable opportunities for men and women
Established under Article 8 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU)
The European Opportunity Fund (EGF) is an EU-wide fund designed to support measures to promote equal opportunities and equal opportunities for women and men in the labour market.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) 2015/849 of 20 December 2015 establishing the European Opportunities Facility (EGFD) and repealing Regulation (EC) No 182/2011.
SUMMARY
The EGF is a European Union-wide financial instrument designed to help small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) and social partners in the fight against social exclusion and discrimination based on sex, racial or ethnic origin, religion or belief, disability, age or sexual orientation.
WHAT DOES THE REGULATION DO?
The regulation establishes the EGF, which is funded by the EU’s general budget.
It aims to:
promote the development of sustainable, inclusive and inclusive labour markets;
encourage self-employment and enterprise creation, including through the establishment of cooperatives.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that the specific measures receiving financial support do not receive other financial support from the EU budget. The measures should not replace passive social protection measures.
The measures should be compatible with the transition towards a resource-efficient and sustainable economy and should not be incompatible with the social protection system.
In addition, the measures should:
contribute to the creation of a sustainable labour market;
support the creation and diversification of labour market activities;
provide information, communication and publicity to the beneficiaries and the public;
raise awareness of the benefits of the measures;
ensure that social partners are involved in the application procedure;
be transparent and accountable to the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a long-term strategy to tackle social exclusion, poverty and social exclusion.
For more information, see:
‘Gender equality and social inclusion’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Social exclusion: where a person is treated less favourably than a person of the same sex because of his or her gender, race, colour, religion, sexual orientation, disability or age.

Summary 63:
Agreement between the European Commission and the EU countries on trade in goods
Agreement with the European Community
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2017/852 on the signing, on behalf of the European Union, and provisional application of the EU-EU Trade Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE AGREEMENT?
The decision approves the agreement between the EU and its Member States, the European Economic Area (EEA) countries and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) on the free movement of goods.
The agreement replaces the 2014 agreement.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EU and EFTA agree to:
cooperate on a number of issues, including:
trade in goods and services;
protection of intellectual property rights;
promotion of sustainable development;
reciprocal access to the EU’s internal market;
the protection of workers’ rights and the environment;
encouraging cooperation between EU countries’ authorities and non-EU countries;
strengthening the role of EU countries in the fight against organised crime and corruption;
protecting human rights and fundamental freedoms;
ensuring the protection of the environment and human rights of workers and workers;
supporting the development of sustainable economic and social development.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The Agreement entered into force on 1 January 2018.
It has applied since 1 January 2019.

The EFTA and EU countries are signatories to the Agreement on Trade in Goods and Services (2014 Agreement).
For more information, see:
European Free Trade Agreement (European Commission).
KEY TERMS

Summary 64:
Agreement between the European Parliament and the Council on budgetary discipline and cooperation between the EU institutions and the European Union Recovery Instrument (EURI Regulation)
Agreement with the Council and the EU Institutions on budgetary disciplines and cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/2023/EU on the signing, on behalf of the EU, and provisional application of the Agreement between the Parliament and Council on the implementation of the annual budgetary procedure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to implement budgetary discipline, to improve the functioning of the budgetary procedure and cooperation on budgetary matters as well as to ensure sound financial management.
KEY POINTS
The decision adopts the agreement between the Commission and the institutions.
The aim of the agreement is to:
ensure that the EU budget is implemented in accordance with the EU’s rules of fiscal discipline;
establish a cooperation and a roadmap towards the introduction, over the period of the multiannual financial framework 2021-2027, of new own resources that are sufficient to cover the repayment of the European EU Recovery Instrument established under Council Regulation 2020/94 (1) (the EURI Regulation);
establish the European Globalisation Adjustment Fund (EGAF) to provide financial support to EU countries that have benefited from the European Stability Mechanism (ESM);
improve the protection of the budget against fraud and irregularities;
improve control and audit of the EURI;
promote cooperation and coordination between the institutions on development policy issues.
In addition, the agreement provides for the establishment of a Conciliation Committee to ensure the proper functioning of budgetary matters.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The Agreement entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Union budget is the main source of revenue for the EU as a whole. It is financed by EU countries’ own resources.

Summary 66:
Sanctions against euro-area countries that fail to comply with the Stability and Growth Pact (SGP)
Sanctions targeting euro area countries that do not meet the medium-term budgetary objective
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 — sanctions against euro area Member States whose currency is the euro
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that Member States who do not respect the medium term budgetary objectives of the European Union (EU) do not suffer excessive financial penalties for not complying with the euro area’s financial rules.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to Member States which are not members of the Single Supervisory Mechanism (SSM).
It does not apply to:
non-euro-area Member States;
euro area countries which do not use the euro; or
Member States which have not adopted the euro.
It is based on Article 126(6) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) which allows the adoption of specific measures which go beyond the provisions applicable to all Member States, for the purpose of ensuring the proper functioning of the economic and monetary union (e.g. the prevention part of the SGP).
The European Commission may, on grounds of exceptional economic circumstances or following a reasoned request by the Member State concerned addressed to the Commission, recommend that the Council reduce the amount of the non-interest-bearing deposit or cancel it.
The Commission must investigate the putative misrepresentations taking into account any comments submitted by the EU country concerned and take into account the seriousness and duration of the misrepresentation. The Commission must submit a report to the Council and to the European Parliament and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) within 10 days of receiving the report.
If the Commission considers that the Member States concerned have not complied with the SGS, it may impose fines on them. The fines must be effective, dissuasive and proportionate to the nature, seriousness and length of the alleged misrepresentation and should not be disproportionate to the seriousness of the offence.
Member State sanctions
The Member State which is not a member of the SSM is subject to sanctions based on the preventive part of Article 126 of the TFEU. These sanctions may include:
a fine of up to €500,000;
a ban on interest-bearing deposits with the European Commission;
suspension of the right to interest on the deposit or cancellation of the deposit;
restrictions on the right of access to the banking system;
the right of the Commission to carry out investigations into the alleged misappropriation of funds.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Preventive measures: measures which aim to prevent the excessive use of the euro by Member States that do, or are likely to, fail to meet their medium- and long-term fiscal objectives.
* Monetary policy: measures adopted by the European Central Bank (ECB) to ensure the stability and effectiveness of the monetary union and to maintain the euro as the single currency.

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 on population and housing data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the collection and transmission of statistics on the European Union’s (EU) population* and housing* populations and housing*.
It aims to improve the quality and comparability of EU statistics.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must submit to the European Commission (Eurostat) the validated data and metadata in electronic form.
Eurostat then forwards the data to the Commission (EU).
The data are collected by Eurostat using the methodology, definitions and the programme of the statistical data and the metadata.
Each EU country must ensure that the data are reliable and comparable at EU level and that they are collected in accordance with the principles of impartiality, objectivity and scientific independence, as well as transparency, reliability, relevance, cost-effectiveness and statistical confidentiality.
The European Commission is assisted by the European Statistical Programme Committee (ESPC).
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?

the creation of the European Statistics Office (ESO);
the introduction of the Eurostat statistical programme;
the establishment of a European Statistical System (EuroStatistical Programme Committee.

Summary 68:
Rules of Procedure for the 9th parliamentary term (2019-2022) — rules of procedure for the European Parliament and of the Council of the European Union (EU)
Rules of Procedures for the nine-yearly Parliament and the European Council of 25 July 2019
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on the Rules of Procedure of the ninth parliamentary term
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the implementation of the procedures for the ninth term of the EU’s Parliament, which runs from 1 July 2019 to 31 December 2020.
It aims to ensure that the procedures are consistent with the rules adopted by the High Level Group on Gender Equality and Diversity on 11 April 2018.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the work of the Parliament, the Council, the Commission, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Committee on Industry, Research and Energy;
the committees responsible for:
research and innovation;
industry, research and energy;
science and technology;
environmental and sustainable development;
health and social policy;
transport;
justice and home affairs;
education and social inclusion;
public procurement;
economic and social affairs; and
the environment.
Procedure
The procedure applies to the following:
procedures for drawing up the estimates for the work of Parliament;
legislative acts;
reports on the work carried out by the Commission;
proceedings of the committee on industry, research, innovation and social development; and the environment;
budgetary measures;
statutes of the Commission and the Council;
exchanges of information between committees;
adoption of reports by committees and subcommittees;
approval of estimates by the Council and the Commission.
Duties of delegations
Each Member of Parliament is responsible for sending a delegation to each EU country to meet with the European Commission. The political composition of the delegation must correspond to the composition of Parliament by political groups.
Delegations must:
meet at least once a year;
notify the Commission of their position;
be accompanied by a copy of the draft estimates;
meet with the Commission at least twice a year.
Requests to European Agencies
Each EU country must submit a request to the Commission for a report on the functioning of its institutions. The Commission must reply to the request within 30 days of receiving it.
The Commission must also reply to any request from the EU country in which the request is made.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation has applied since 1 July 2013 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
The rules of Procedure have been adapted to take account of the guidelines on gender equality and diversity adopted in April 2018 by the high-level group on gender-neutral language in its documents.
For more information, see:
‘Rules of procedure’ (European Parliament).

KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the EDPS.
The EDPS’s tasks are to:
ensure that data protection is respected by EU institutions and bodies;
protect the rights and fundamental freedoms of the natural persons concerned;
assist the European Commission in its investigations and enforcement of data protection offences;
monitor the application of EU data protection rules and regulations;
provide the Commission with information on data protection violations;
cooperate with national authorities and judicial and administrative authorities in EU countries and international organisations;
act as a mediator between the Commission and national authorities, judicial or administrative authorities and national data protection authorities.

Summary 70:
EU’s own resources — rules for the collection and management of own resources
EU's own resources— rules for collecting and managing own resources (European Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 609/2014 — rules on own resources for the European Commission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on how the European Union (EU) collects and manages its own resources.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ own resources contribute to the development of the EU budget.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to the European Economic Area (EEA) countries, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland, Norway and Switzerland.
Each EU country must draw up a budget for the year starting on 1 January 2015.
The Commission may call on EU countries to provisionally provide the difference between the overall assets and the cash resource requirements, in proportion (‘pro rata’) to the estimated budget revenue of each of them.
EU countries may also call on the Commission to provide a maximum amount that the Commission may annually call from a specific category of own resource.
Rules for the calculation of the own resources and methods for their calculation are set out in the regulation.
This regulation does not affect the rules on the use of own funds by the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLACE INTO FORCE?
 
   
It has applied since 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for collecting, managing and managing EU own resources since 1 January 2016.
For more information, see:
‘EU own resources’ on the EU's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Own resources: EU countries' own resources that they collect and manage on their own behalf.

Summary 71:
EU rules on tyres — minimum requirements for fuel efficiency and wet grip of tyres
EU rules for tyres — Minimum requirements for fuels and tyres
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — minimum fuel efficiency requirements for tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down minimum requirements to improve the fuel efficiency of tyres.
It aims to ensure that tyres comply with the EU’s 2020 climate and energy policy framework.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all types of tyres, including:
road tyres;
trailers;
trucks;
motorcycles;
bicycles;
cars and light commercial vehicles;
road mobile vehicles; and
trams and heavy commercial vehicles.
EU countries must ensure that:
tire labels and product information sheets are accurate and clearly displayed;
the tyre label and information sheet are clearly displayed on the vehicle and on the product information sheet;
all tyres are tested and certified according to the requirements laid down in Regulation (EC) No 661/2009 on wet grip and snow grip;
instructions and technical documentation are provided on the label and on products information sheets;
manufacturers, importers and distributors are responsible for ensuring the accuracy of the tyre labels and information sheets and for the conformity assessment of tyres and for complying with the regulation.
Manufacturers and importers must also ensure that the tyre label is clearly displayed and illustrated on their vehicles and on their products.
inform the European Commission of the type of tyre they make available to the public.
they must also inform the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the best practice in the tyre industry.
The Commission must review the regulation in the light of technological progress and present a report on its implementation by 31 December 2022.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
  
It has applied since 31 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve fuel efficiency, such as:
the introduction of a new EU-wide fuel efficiency target of 20% by 2020;
a new EU fuel efficiency standard for motor vehicles by 2020.

Summary 72:
European Union funding for customs controls (EU 2021-2027 instrument) (European Commission)
European Union (EU) 2021-2020 instrument
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021/2027 — EU funding for Customs Controls
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the objectives of the Instrument, the budget for the period 2021 2027, the forms of EU funding and the rules for providing such funding.
It aims to contribute to adequate and equivalent results of customs controls.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The instrument covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027. It does not apply to:
the EU’s external borders;
the internal borders of the European Economic Area (EEA) and the Schengen area;
EU countries’ customs controls;
customs controls with equivalent results.
Objectives
The objective of the instrument is to: ensure that customs controls are effective, efficient, proportionate and proportionate; andrecover the costs of fraud, loss, wrongly paid or incorrectly used.
Implementation
The aim of the regulation is to ensure that the instruments are implemented and that EU funding is used effectively.
The regulation lays down a number of measures to achieve these objectives, including:
promotion, monitoring, monitoring and evaluation;
monitoring and evaluation of the implementation of the instruments;
organisation and coordination of work programmes;
reciprocal access to the instruments and grants;
recovery of funds;
combining award, complementarity and combined funding;
promoting cooperation between EU countries;
exchanges of information and best practice;
cooperation between EU and non-EU countries.
EU funding
The instruments provide for the following forms of funding:
grants, awards and combined grants; and
exemptions.
Funding
The Instrument is funded by the general budget of the EU. It is divided into 3 types of grants:
general grants, which are based on the total amount of goods imported into the EU and the number of persons, goods exported, goods imported and goods exported from the EU;
specific grants, such as grants for the prevention, detection, correction and investigation of irregularities, including fraud, to the recovery of funds lost or wrongly paid, and to the imposition of administrative penalties.
Awards
The European Commission may award certain types of funding, including grants based on:
a specific type of award;
a combination of grants; or
a grant based on a specific category of goods.
Exemptions
The Commission may exclude from the award certain categories of grants the following categories of goods: alcoholic beverages, tobacco, tobacco-related products, foodstuffs, pharmaceuticals, machinery and equipment, chemicals, machinery used for the manufacture of weapons, explosives, explosives and weapons of mass destruction, and machinery used by the armed forces, for example.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 20 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘EU customs controls’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Customs controls: the control of goods entering and exiting the EU's external borders in order to ensure both safety and security.

Summary 74:
European Joint Undertaking for the development and use of top-class technologies and infrastructures in the field of high-performance computing (EuroHPC) (European Partnerships for the Development and Use of Top-Class Technologies and Infrastructure)
European joint undertaking for research and innovation in the area of high performance computing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the European Joint undertaking for Research and Innovation in the Area of High Performance Computing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EuroHPC joint undertaking (JUB) which aims to develop and use top-performing technologies and infrastructure to meet the demanding requirements of European scientific, industrial and public sector users.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the JUB is to:
contribute to the creation of a European supercomputer or a quantum computer;
provide the European Union (EU) with financial support for the acquisition and ownership of supercomputers and quantum simulators;

promote cooperation between the EU and participating countries in the development, use and development of these systems;
facilitate access to the supercomputer and the infrastructure.
Joint Undertaking
The JUB’s activities are governed by a Governing Board composed of representatives of the participating countries and chaired by the Executive Director.
The Joint Board is responsible for:
monitoring and evaluating the implementation of the joint undertaking;
preparing a multiannual strategic programme;
drafting the annual work programme.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLYORKER DOCUMENT
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Joint Overseeing Centre (JAC) is a joint undertaking set up under the Horizon Europe programme. It is funded by the European Commission and the European Research Council (ERC) and is managed by the JAC.
For more information, see:
‘Joint undertaking’ on the EU's Horizon Europe website.

Summary 75:
EU climate targets for 2020-2030
EU carbon dioxide emissions
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2020/2030 — EU climate targets
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out the EU’s 2020–2030 climate targets.
It aims to tackle climate change and to deliver on the implementation of the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must set national targets for reducing their greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by 2020. These targets are based on the long-term temperature goal of 2°C above pre-industrial levels (the Paris Agreement) and on a climate-neutrality objective.
The 2020 targets are set out in Annex I of the directive.
By 30 June 2021, the Commission must review relevant EU legislation in order to enable the EU to achieve the target. This is without prejudice to the review of the relevant Union legislation.
In addition, EU countries must review their national measures to ensure that they are in line with the 2020 targets. These measures must include:
a national strategy to reduce GHG emissions by 30 % by 2020 compared to 1990 levels;
a climate neutrality objective by 2030;
measures to reduce greenhouse gas emissions by 20 % below 1990 levels compared to 2050 levels.
National strategies must be based on a number of factors, such as:
the EU's 2020 target;
the 2030 climate neutrality objective;
carbon capture and storage (CCS) and the 2030 climate-reduction objective
target;
national measures to reduce emissions by 40 % compared with 1990 levels by 2030.
A national strategies must also include a national climate action plan and a national action plan for 2020 and 2030, as well as a national strategy for 2030, which must include measures to tackle CO2 emissions by 2050.
EU governments must also:
ensure that their national strategies and national action plans are aligned with the EU's 2030 targets;
take the necessary measures to meet the 2020 target and the 2020 climate neutrality goal;
review their strategies every 5 years;
assess the progress they have made and assess the need to make further progress;
provide the Commission with scientific advice on climate change.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive applies from 30 June 2020.
BACKGROUND
The EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle the climate change caused by human activity.
For more information, see:
‘The EU 2020 climate targets’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Climate change: the warming of the planet resulting from human activity and its effects on the Earth's climate.
ACT
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 of 30 May 2019 on the EU 2020–2020 climate targets (OJ L 157, 31.5.2019, pp. 1-2)
RELATED ACTS
Council Directive (EU), Euratom of 30 June 2015 on the adoption of the EU 2030 climate targets and the EU 2050 climate targets, and repealing Council Decision (Euratom) 2015/853 of 30 April 2015 on implementing Council Decision 2014/854/EU on the Paris Climate Agreement (Official Journal C 202, 30.6.2015, pp.).

Summary 79:
Protection of aromatised wine products
Protected aromatisation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — rules applicable to the definition, description, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of certain aromatized wine products and the protection of trademarks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the protection, description and labelling of certain aromatic-tasting wine products.
It aims to ensure that the products covered by the regulation comply with the EU’s international obligations concerning the quality of wine.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products produced in the EU and imported into the EU from non-EU countries. It does not apply to wines produced in non-European Union countries.
Protection
The legislation applies to the category of products which are marketed as ‘Aromatised wines’, ‘Extra Dry’ or ‘extra-dry’ and ‘Wine-based aperitifs’.
The protection of these products is based on the geographical indication (GI) which is used by the European Commission to describe and describe the products and to describe the characteristics of each category of product.
EU countries may apply the GI to products which do not fall within the scope of the regulation.
Terms used
The term ‘aperitif’ may be used only to describe products which fall within its scope.
Products which are labelled ‘wines’ are not protected by the GI if they do not meet the following the conditions laid down in Regulation (EU countries’ national laws and regulations.
Exceptions
Products labelled with the GI are protected if they are:
in the case of products with a sugar content of less than 30 grams per litre and
for products which have a minimum total alcoholic strength by volume of 15 % vol.;
in cases where the GI is used to describe a product which does not conform to the requirements laid down by Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the quality control of wine products (see summary).
The GI may also be used for products which meet the requirements set out in the annex to the regulation, such as wines with a lower sugar content than 30 mg/litre or wines with more than 30 % alcohol by volume.
GEOGRAPHICAL INDICATIONS
The European Commission may designate a geographical indication for a product if it considers that the product meets the conditions set out by the legislation. The protection of the GI may be extended by 2 months on the initiative of the European Parliament or the Council.
Where a product is protected by a GI, it must be accompanied by a description of the product and the description of its characteristics. The description must include:
the name of the producer;
the geographical indication;
a description of how the product is produced;
descriptions of the production method;
an indication of the sales denomination;
any other information which may be necessary for the identification of the products;
details concerning the sugar content and the alcohol content by volume;
all other information that may be needed for the description and presentation.
A GI may not be used in combination with other information provided by the producer.
Producers may lodge an application for protection only for the products which they produce. They may also lodge applications for protection for products that they produce for other categories of products for which they have not applied for protection.
If the protection is refused, the product may be subject to a marketing ban.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/856 has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Aromatic wines (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барскабадарълъта бодарка (OJ L 157, 1.4.2018, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EJ L 167, 1) have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 80:
Restrictive measures for the conservation of stocks of certain stocks of fish and crustaceans in the North Atlantic Ocean
Restricting the fishing of certain species of fish
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the conservation measures for stocks of species of fishes and crustacesans
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down conservation measures to protect stocks of the stocks of some fish species in the north Atlantic Ocean (NEAFC).
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules for the sustainable management of these stocks, based on the best available scientific, technical and economic advice, taking into account available scientific and technical advice, including, where relevant, reports drawn up by the Scientific, Technical and Economic Committee for Fisheries (STECF) and other advisory bodies, as well as any advice received from advisory councils.
It does not apply to:
fishing in Norway, Iceland, Norway, the Faroe Islands, Greenland, Iceland and Norway;
targeted fishing in the Baltic Sea;
fisheries in the Black Sea and in the Gulf of Finland;
recursions into the waters of the North Sea.
The regulations apply to the following species:
redfish,
common sole,
solea solea,
slab sole*,
solitary sole*, and
common common sole.
TACs
The TACs are set out in the regulation’s annexes to the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that:
the maximum allowable catch (TAC) for each species is not less than 0.
1.5 tonnes per fishing vessel per fishing season;
the total catches of vessels not targeting this species may not exceed 1.
Each year, EU countries must submit a report to the European Commission on the implementation of the TAC. This report must include:
a description of the species targeted;
a list of the vessels involved;
an estimate of the number of vessels involved in the fishery;
information on the number and type of vessels participating in the fishing season.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* TAC: total allowable catch.
* Total allowable catch: the total number of tonnes of fish caught by a vessel per year.
ACT

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?

The European Commission may suspend or cancel the assistance if it considers that the assistance is no longer necessary or that the conditions for its implementation are no longer met. In such cases, it must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for the suspension or cancellation.
