Summary 82:
EU system for control, inspection and enforcement of IUU activities in the Mediterranean swordfish fishery (2013-2020)
EU system of control, inspections and enforcement in the Caribbean
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — EU system for controlling, inspecting and enforcing IUU in the Atlantic tuna fishery
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to ensure that the EU’s system of controls and inspections is in place to prevent and combat the illegal fishing of swordfish and bluefin tuna by preventing and combating IUU (i.e. the illegal taking of fish by non-EU nationals).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fishing for swordfish, including swordfish caught outside the European Union (EU);
bluefin tuna caught within the EU;
redfin tuna and Atlantic tuna caught inside the EU.
It does not apply to tuna caught in the Black Sea.
Control measures
EU countries must:
ensure that vessels carrying out IUU fishing comply with the rules of the Convention on the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (the ICCAT Convention);
monitor and report to the European Commission on the implementation of the control measures;
establish a system of inspections and control measures to ensure compliance with the CFP;
monitor the operation of vessels and ensure that vessels that are involved in IUU do not exceed the national quota;
take more than 80% of the national quotas;
carry out inspections to check that vessels are complying with the relevant rules;
issue vessels with an official identification document;
notify the Commission if they believe that a vessel has been involved in an IUU offence;
use a vessel to which has not complied with the controls.
Monitoring and reporting
Each year, EU countries must send data on catches to the Commission on a weekly basis. The data must include:
the number and type of vessel;
the total number of vessels involved in the fishing;
total number of catches;
record of vessels;
details of the number of persons on board;
number of vessels registered in each EU country;
a list of vessels that have been identified as being involved.
Each EU country must also:
issue each vessel with a unique identification number and a unique country specific number and be tamper proof;
check that the vessel is registered in the country of registration;
keep a logbook of all fishing activities;
assess the number, type and weight of the fish caught;
report to the EU on the annual fishing plans submitted by the EU countries.
The Commission must also ensure that EU countries:
keep records of the vessels and the data they collect;
provide the Commission with a copy of the fishing plans and the annual report;
inform the Commission of any vessel that is suspected of being involved in illegal fishing.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Union is party to the International Convention for the Conservation and Trade in Tunas, which aims to protect Atlantic tuna stocks.
For more information, see:
‘Fishing for Mediterranean Swordfish’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* IUU: fishing for fish that is not caught for commercial purposes.
* Bluefin tuna: the largest tuna species in the world.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 of 20 December 2009 on the control and inspection system for the conservation and sustainable management of Atlantic tuna fisheries (OJ L 321, 31.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
Council Decision 2013/856/EU of 27 December 2013 on the application of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 182/2011 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the Union system for Control, Inspection and Enforcement of Illegal Fishing in the Fisheries of the Atlantic and repealing Council Regulation (Euratom) 2016/1627 on the protection of Atlantic Bluefin Tunas and the European Convention on Catching and Processing of Bluefin Tuna (ECCAT Convention).
last update 14.02.2018



Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework (MFF) for the period 2014-2020
Multiannional financial framework for the years 2014-20
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 establishing a multiannual framework for cohesion policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and ceilings for the European Union’s (EU's) Multiannual Financial Framework, which is designed to help EU countries prepare and implement medium-term investments.
KEY POINTS
The MFF is the main instrument of the EU's cohesion policy, which aims to support EU countries' efforts to strengthen their economic and social cohesion.
The regulation sets out rules for the use of the MFF, including the respect of own resources (i.e. EU countries’ own resources).
It also sets out procedures for the management of the funds.
Scope
The framework covers the period from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2020.
It does not apply to certain specific instruments, such as the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Solidarity Fund or the European Globalisation Adjustment Fund.
Funding
The European Union (EU), as a whole, has a budget of €7.2 trillion for the 2014-2019 period.
For the period 2017-2020, the European Commission is responsible for managing the funds and ensuring that they are:
contributed by the EU countries;
spent according to their own resources;
reimbursed by the Commission;
in accordance with the EU budget rules;
provided by the European Stability Mechanism (ESMTF) and the European Pillar of Social Rights (PSRSPR);
provided for by the Cohesion Fund (Cohesion Fund).
The Commission must ensure that:
EU countries comply with the ceilings set out in the regulation;
allocating the funds in proportion to the needs of the country concerned;
providing the Commission with all the information it needs to make the appropriate adjustments to the ceilings;
keeping the Commission informed of the progress made on the payments made;
notifying the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission of any measures taken to adjust the ceilings.
In addition, the Commission must:
monitor the implementation of cohesion policy;
make the necessary adjustments to ensure that the required adjustments are spread in equal proportions over the years 2017-20;
ensure that the ceilings are not exceeded;
cooperate in good faith with the institutions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2013. It had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has had to adapt to the changes in economic and budgetary conditions since the Lisbon Treaty came into force on 1 January 2004. This has led to the need for a longer-term financial framework. The MFF was set up in 2014 to provide for a more predictable and predictable period of funding for EU countries.
This regulation is one of a series of measures designed to ensure the proper functioning of the European cohesion policy. The other measures include:
the creation of a European Regional Investment Bank (ERIB);
the European Investment Bank for Europe (EIB); and
the EIB for the United Kingdom (1) and Ireland (1).
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ on the Commission's website.

Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1212/2013
1.1.2014
-
OJ L 136 of 1.3.2013

Summary 84:
European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFAF)
Eastern Mediterranean and Antarctic Fund for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas and Atlantic Aquaculture (EMFAC)
Established under Regulation (EU) 2021/1060, the European Maritime and Food Chain Fund is the European Union’s fisheries and aquaculture fund. Its main objective is to promote the sustainable use of the oceans and the sustainable management of the seas and oceans.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 596/2011/EEC of 21 December 2011 establishing the European Mediterranean and Atlantic Fund for Conservation of the Atlantic Ocean and the Mediterranean (EMAFAC).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The European Maritime, Fisheries and Aquacultural Fund is an EU-funded body whose main aim is to:
promote the sustainable exploitation of the marine environment;
improve the health, safety and working conditions of fishery workers;
ensure sustainable fishing;
contribute to the protection of marine ecosystems;
support the sustainable development of the fisheries sector.
KEY POINTS
Funding
The EMFAF is funded by the EU budget and is managed by the European Commission.
The EU contributes €2.5 billion to the fund. This contribution is based on a weighted average of the EU countries’ gross domestic product (GDP) per tonne of fish caught during the previous year.
It covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2023.
Specific rules apply to the following the entry into force of the regulation:
EU countries into the EMFAFCDATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCELEBRATION

Summary 85:
Convention on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish and Highly Migratory Fish Stocks and the Convention on the International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (IATTC)
Convention of the International Tribunal for the Law of the Sea on the conservation and management of the living resources of the sea
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the Convention relating to the Conservation, Management and Regulation of Fishing in the Area of the United Nations Convention on Conservation and of the Management of the Living Resources of the Seas (ICTY)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that fishing vessels used or intended for use for the purposes of fishing in the area covered by the IATTC Convention comply with the principles and rules laid down in the convention.
KEY POINTS
The convention applies to all fishing vessels using or intended to use for fishing in areas of the world’s oceans covered by it.
The regulation applies to EU fishing vessels.
It does not apply to vessels owned or operated by EU countries.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to inspect and verify the vessels’ well-inspected and sealed vessels before using them for the purpose of fishing.
Fishing vessels must:
not use any fishing gear that could harm the marine environment;
not be used to catch sharks, porpoises, skates or skates;
use a purse seabirds;
keep a list of species of fish that may be caught;
inform the International Commission of the species they intend to catch;
ensure that the species are not caught and that they are surrendered to the International Committee for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT);
keep records of catches;
report to the Commission on catches and the number of sharks caught and surrendered.
FROM WHEN DOES THE CONVENTION APPLY?
The Convention applies from 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a contracting party to the Convention.

Summary 86:
Delegated acts on the harmonisation of EU legislation
Delegation of EU laws
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the European Parliament, the Council, the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee
Decision (EU) 2016/852 on the signing, on behalf of the European Union, of the Agreement between the EU and the three European Parliament and the Council on the Harmonisation of European Union legislation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that EU legislation is harmonised in order to improve the functioning of the EU’s internal market.
It aims to:
improve the efficiency of the internal market;
reduce administrative burdens for businesses and consumers;
ensure that legislation is compatible with EU law;
promote cooperation between EU countries’ legal systems;
encourage the exchange of information and experience between EU and non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The legislation covered by the agreement applies to all EU legislation, whether it is new or existing.
The agreements cover:
new legislation;
delegislative acts adopted by EU countries;
existing legislation adopted by the Commission;
the transposition of existing legislation; and
the implementation of delegated acts.
Exchanges of information
The European Commission will consult experts designated by each EU country in the preparation of draft delegated acts and draft legislation.
EU countries must provide the Commission with all relevant information on existing legislation, including:
the existence, scale and consequences of a problem and the question whether or not EU action is needed;
whether or not the EU action should be taken;
a description of the impact of the problem;
how the legislation should be implemented;
measures to prevent or minimise the negative effects of the legislation on the economy and society;
exchanges of experience between the Commission and EU countries on the implementation of legislation and its transposition.
In addition, the Commission will also:
provide the EU countries with information on the impact assessments carried out by the European Regulatory Scrutiny Board;
make available to the Commission the results of these impact assessments;
assess whether the legislation does not need to be amended;
prepare a report for the Commission on the draft legislation and on its implementation.
Implementation
The Commission will monitor, on a regular basis throughout the year, how the agreement is being implemented.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Agreement entered into force on the day of its signature.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:

* Delegated act: a delegated act which authorises the Commission to adopt a new law in another EU country.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DELEGATED ACT?

It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries and the European Commission.
KEY POINTS

keep the information they collect in a publicly accessible database.

In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
the data they collect are not used for any other purpose other than for the purpose of collecting information on the EU citizens;
the information collected is not used to target EU citizens, such as for political purposes;
there is no need to disclose personal data;
they are not to be used for other purposes other than to identify EU citizens.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?

surveys,
administrative sources,
information concerning the placing on the market and use of certain pesticides taking into account the obligations pursuant to Article 67 of Regulation (EC) No 1107/2009, or
a combination of these means, including statistical estimation procedures on the basis of expert judgements or models,
the use of these methods,
data collected by the Commission or by EU countries’ competent authorities.
the Commission may choose the reference period at any time of 5 years.

Summary 89:
Active substances — plant protection
Active substances— plant protection	ACT
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the application of the laws of the Member States relating to the use of active substances
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down rules for the application and authorisation of substances for the manufacture of plant protection products.
It aims to ensure that substances used in plants are safe and effective.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances used to protect plants against the adverse effects of pesticides. It does not apply to substances intended for use in foodstuffs, such as food additives, which are covered by Directive 91/414/EEC (see summary).
It does not cover substances used for the production of food additives.
EU countries may apply different criteria for the first approval of an active substance than for the second approval of a substance.
The substances covered by the regulation are:
active substances, safeners, synergists and co-formulants*;
safer substances*; and
co-formulaants* and synergists*.
The European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) is responsible for evaluating the applications for the approval of substances.
Examination
The competent authority of an EU country examines the application for authorisation and decides within 12 months of receiving it whether the application meets the requirements.
If the competent authority in that country considers that the substance meets the criteria, it authorises the substance to be placed on the market. If not, it withdraws the substance from the application. If the competent authorities in the EU country examining the application decide that the application does not comply with the regulation.
An EU country may refuse to authorise a substance if it considers that:
the substance does not meet the requirements for the protection of plants;
it is likely to increase the risk of adverse reactions to the substance;
there is a risk that a substance may be used in combination with a substance that is not intended for plant protection; or
there are differences in the composition of the substance and the safety profile of the product.
A substance may not be used as a replacement for a substance which is already authorised.
In addition, EU countries may not prohibit the import of substances from non-EU countries which are not covered by this regulation, provided that the substances are not used in products intended for human consumption.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATOR APPLY?
Directive 2005/43/EC has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
ACT

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

Cooperation

improve the conservation and management of marine ecosystems;
encourage the sustainable use of marine life;
support the sustainable development of Mediterranean fisheries.
protect the Mediterranean biodiversity;
provide a forum for international cooperation.

conservation, conservation and rational management;
environmental protection;
promotion of sustainable fishing practices;
integration of sustainable fisheries into national and regional policies and programmes;
protection of migratory species;
cooperation with the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and the International Labour Organisation (ILO).

Summary 92:
European patent with unitary effect — licensing and enforcement of patents in different EU countries (until 31 December 2021)
European patent (EU) No 1260/2012 on licensing and enforcing patents with single-piece patent protection (until 30 December 2021).
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It aims to ensure that patents granted with a single piece of intellectual property (IP) have the same legal status in all EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The European Patent Office (EPO) has the task of enforcing patents granted in more than one EU country.
The regulation applies to patents that have a unitary character. These patents may be licensed in respect of the whole or part of the territories of the participating EU countries and may only be limited, transferred or revoked, or lapse.
Unitary patent protection may be requested for any European patent granted on or after the date of application of this regulation.
EU countries must ensure that requests for patent protection are filed with the EPO within one month of publication of the mention of the grant in the European Patent Bulletin and ensure that such requests are submitted in the language of the EU country where the patent was granted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve patent protection. These include:
Regulation (EC) No 882/2006 on the application of patents to the EU market;
Directive 2014/35/EU on patent licensing and enforceability of patents;
Decision 2014/34/36/EU setting up a European Patent Licensing Committee;
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/852 on the licensing of patents and related intellectual property rights.

Summary 94:
EU rules on international trade in goods and services — rules on origin and origin of goods and service contracts (trade in goods)
Trade in goods & services — measures on origin & origin
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — rules to ensure the effective exercise of the EU's rights under international trade agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s rights are respected when it comes to international trade.
It lays down rules on the import and export of goods from non-EU countries to the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
goods and services originating outside the EU;
services originating in the EU and imported into the EU on the territory of an EU country;
contracting authorities or entities receiving tenders;
information and guarantees provided by tenderers as regards the origin of the goods or services.
EU countries must apply normal precautions and exercise due diligence when assessing information and guarantees when assessing tenderers’ claims as regards:
the origin of a good or service;
the conditions under which a good is supplied;
whether it has been manufactured in a non-European Union country; and
the nature of the contract.
Implementing measures
The European Commission must adopt implementing measures to ensure compliance with the relevant international trade rules. These measures may include:
suspension, modification and repeal of measures;
restrictions on the supply of goods, services or services originating in a third country.
The Commission must keep the European Parliament and the European Council regularly informed of international developments that may lead to situations requiring the adoption of measures under this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLYORKER INFORMATION
The EU country concerned must provide the Commission with information on the origin and destination of goods. The supplier of information may request that the information supplied be treated as confidential. The Commission must take the input received into account.
Information received must be used only for the purpose for which it was requested and must not be used for any other purpose than to obtain information for which the supplier has a legitimate interest.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that:
information provided by suppliers must be accurate, complete and complete, and that it does not contain any false or misleading information;
tenders must not provide false or incomplete information, provided that it is for a legitimate commercial purpose;
suppliers must be able to prove that they have been properly informed of the origin or destination of the good or services they provide;
in the case of public procurement, EU countries’ public authorities must take into account input from EU countries' public authorities in the preparation of draft implementing acts.
BACKGROUND
The legislation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the functioning of the internal market and to protect EU citizens’ economic interests.
For more information, see:
International trade — EU rules (European Commission).

Summary 97:
Border surveillance of the external borders of the Schengen maritime area (SESO)
Budgetary rules for border surveillance of sea external borders
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — rules for the coordination of border surveillance at the external border of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for EU-wide border surveillance in the maritime area.
It aims to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ territorial sea and contiguous sea borders.
EU countries are required to cooperate with each other to:
detect vessels suspected of breaching their territorial sea or contiguous maritime borders;
cooperate with the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) and the European Border and Coast Guard;
organise and coordinate search and rescue operations;
ensure the safety of persons on board vessels;
take all appropriate measures to ensure the health and safety of the persons concerned;
carry out searches and rescues;
prepare for and coordinate rescue operations.
Each participating unit must include at least one person with basic first-aid training.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s maritime surveillance policy is governed by the Schenevängen Borders Code (see summary).
For more information, see:
Border surveillance (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Maritime surveillance: the surveillance of maritime borders by EU countries and the use of ships, aircraft and other means of transport.
* Border surveillance: measures taken to prevent, detect, investigate and prosecute persons who cross the border illegally.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/856 of 29 June 2018 laying down rules on the coordination between EU countries for the implementation of the border surveillance measures at the sea external border (OJ L 157, 1.7.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 98:
EU migration and asylum support — specific regulations for EU actions and emergency assistance
EU migrants and asylum assistance — specific Regulations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — specific rules for EU action and humanitarian assistance for migration, asylum and border control
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down specific rules on the EU’s assistance to EU countries and the European Commission in the event of migration and refugees.
It aims to support EU countries’ efforts to cope with the pressure arising from the external borders of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU actions and assistance
The specific regulations provide for:
support for EU countries to respond to the influx of migrants, asylum seekers and refugees and to the management of their external borders;
support to the Commission to implement the work programme;
cooperation between the Commission and EU countries;
assistance to the European Asylum Support Partnership (EASP).
The EASP is a partnership between the EU countries, the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council of the EU and the Commission. It is designed to:
contribute to the prevention and control of illegal immigration;
provide the Commission with information and information on EU actions;
ensure that EU actions are implemented in line with international and human rights law;
promote cooperation between EU countries in the field of migration;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice;
encourage cooperation between national authorities;
raise awareness of the need for EU assistance;
improve cooperation and cooperation;
exchange information on the needs of EU citizens.
The regulation lays down detailed rules on eligibility for the programme.
EU countries are responsible for the implementation of the specific regulations.
EASAPPLY
Each EU country is responsible for implementing the specific regulation. The Commission may adopt, by way of implementing acts, its own implementing acts.
Each year, each EU country must submit a work programme for the period from 1 January to 31 December of the previous year. The work programme must include:
a description of the actions to be undertaken;
a list of the types of actions and measures to be carried out;
details of the tasks to be performed;
the duration of the programme and the budget for each action;
measures to be taken by the Commission;
reports on the progress made by EU countries on the implementation and results of the work programmes;
an annual report on the work of the partnership.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
EU Migration and Asylum Support (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

Summary 100:
Protecting people and goods from transnational threats: EU instrument for combating crime and crime against the environment (EU Instrument for the prevention of transnational crime and terrorism)
Protecting the lives and property of EU citizens and the property of the EU’s citizens and of the European Union (EU) institutions
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — EU instrument to combat transnational crimes and crime of the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union instrument (EU-I instrument) to combat cross-border crime.
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries’ judicial authorities and law enforcement authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to matters that are covered by the Justice programme, as set out in Regulation 1382/2013.
EU countries may not use more than 8 % of their total allocation under the national programme for the maintenance of EU IT systems and national IT systems contributing to the objectives of this regulation.
The instrument may also be used for actions in relation to or in third countries which implement the strategic EU priorities listed in Annex I to this regulation, such as the fight against transnational organised crime, terrorism, corruption, organised crime and money laundering.
Eligible actions
The European Commission is responsible for drawing up the list of actions that are eligible for funding under the EU-wide EU instrument.
These actions are based on the following criteria:
the objective of preventing, detecting, investigating and prosecuting serious crimes,
the prevention and combating terrorism and the financing of organised crime;
the fight against corruption and the promotion of the rule of law and good governance,
cooperation between judicial authorities,
good governance and good law enforcement,
promoting judicial cooperation,
encouraging judicial cooperation and good practice,
strengthening judicial cooperation with law enforcement and judicial cooperation between national authorities and judicial authorities;
cooperating with non-EU countries in order to prevent, investigate, investigate and prosecute serious crimes;
supporting judicial cooperation in criminal matters;
protecting the rights of the victims of crime and their families;
providing assistance to victims and their legal representatives;
raising public awareness of the importance of judicial cooperation;
exercising the right of access to justice for victims and victims’ families.
Funding
The EU instrument is funded by the EU budget. The global resources for the implementation of the instrument are EUR 1 004 million in current prices.
National programmes
The allocation for national programmes calculated on the basis of criteria referred to in Annex III to the regulation is based on current prices and is divided into the following allocations:
8 % of the total allocation for the Maintenance of Union IT systems;
8% for the improvement of the security of EU networks;
10 % for the development of EU information and communication technologies;
12 % for information and communications technologies (ICTs);
12% for information security;
13% for training and mentoring;
14% for legal assistance;
15% for judicial cooperation.
In addition, the European Commission may use up to 8% of its total allocation from the national programmes for actions that contribute to the achievement of the objectives listed in the strategic Union priorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Transnational crime’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom of 20 December 2018 on the European Instrument for combating transnational offences and crimes of the Environment (OJ L 183, 31.12.2018, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 183/2018 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect the Mediterranean stocks of tuna, anchovies and swordfish by limiting the fishing of certain species of fish caught illegally.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules on the EU’s implementing powers.
It does not apply to countries that are not party to the Convention on the Law of the Sea (COSMOS) or that have no competence for the stock in question.
In addition, the regulation does not cover:
non-compliance with international rules on sustainable fishing;
fishing in areas where non-compliance is prohibited, such as the Mediterranean Sea;
the use of fishing gear that does not comply with EU rules on fishing gear;
use of non-targets that are considered to be harmful to the marine environment.

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries (Bilateral Investment Agreements)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — EU-wide rules on bilateral investment agreements with third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the EU’s approach to the negotiation and conclusion of investment agreements* with non-European Union countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must notify the European Commission at least 5 months in advance of any intention to negotiate or conclude a bilateral investment agreement with a third country. The notification must include a copy of those agreements and the relevant information.
The Commission must then inform the other party to the agreement within 90 days of receiving the notification.
If the Commission finds that the agreement does not fulfil the requirements of Article 9(1) and (2) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU), it must authorise the maintenance or entry into force of such an agreement under EU law.
It must also immediately inform the Commission of any request for dispute settlement lodged under the auspices of the agreement as soon as the EU country becomes aware of such a request.
In addition, the Commission must be assisted by a committee for investment agreements set up by the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 10 January 2020.
BACKGROUND

Summary 105:
European situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of the European Union (EU) (European situational picture and common pre-frontier intelligence picture)
European situational overview
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on the European situational awareness & reaction capability of the EU’s external borders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to provide EU countries’ authorities with the infrastructure and tools to improve their situational awareness, reaction and intelligence capability at their external borders to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contribute to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
The directive sets up a European situational picture (ESP) which is composed of information collected from the following sources:
national situational pictures, to the extent required by this directive;
the European Union Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms (EUROSUR);
the Commission, providing strategic information on border control, including shortcomings in the carrying-out of external border control;
EU delegations and offices;
other relevant EU bodies, offices and agencies and international organisations as referred to in the annexes to the directive.
The ESP consists of:
a national coordination centre;
a common European surveillance picture;
an operational layer of the ESP;
common pre-border intelligence picture.
EU countries must:
ensure that:
the ESP is operational in line with EU standards;
identify and react to the needs of EU countries;
use all relevant surveillance tools;
cooperate with the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBSA);
coordinate with the national border control authorities;
coordinate the use of surveillance tools.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYORK INTO FORCE?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
  
For more information, see:
External borders (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* European situational overview: an overview of the situation at the EU's external borders.
* Common pre-Frontier intelligence: a picture of the security situation at EU countries' external borders prior to the entry into force of the Schengen Agreement.
REFERENCESP: a common intelligence picture composed of national situational pictures and a common operational layer.

Summary 107:
Registration of traditional names for agricultural products and rural economy products
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — registration of traditional name for agricultural and rural economies
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/9/EC on the registration of names
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to encourage traditional names to be registered in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules for the use of these names for the identification of products produced by farmers and producers in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to products produced in the territory of the EU and to rural economy.
It does not apply to agricultural products or to the rural economy produced in non-EU countries.
Farmers and producers must register a name for identification purposes without reservation of the name in the country where the products are produced.
They must also be able to correctly identify their products on the marketplace.
The rules for registering a name are laid down in the regulation’s annexes to the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Traditional names’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Traditional name
Traditional name: a name used by farmers or producers to describe products which are produced in a traditional way and which are closely linked to agricultural production or to rural life.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Traditional names: a registered name used to describe a product produced by a farmer or producer in an EU country.
a name used for a product which is closely connected to agricultural or rural economy or to a rural economy

Summary 108:
Single-hull oil tankers — safety and environmental impact
Single-Hull oil tanker safety and environment impact
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 417/2002 on the phasing-out of the single-HULL oil tanker
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) complies with International Maritime Organization (IMO) rules on the design and construction of single hulled oil tanker (SOHO) vessels.
KEY POINTS
The regulation repeals and repeals Regulation (EC/417/2002 which had been amended several times.
It is a recast version of a previous regulation (Decision (EU, Euratom) No 479/2002, which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2002.
The new regulation is based on the MARPOL (Maritime Safety Convention on the Prevention and Control of Pollution from Ships) and the International Maritime Organisation (MARPOL) rules.
This regulation is designed to ensure the safe operation of the EU’s maritime transport market and to ensure a high level of environmental protection.
Scope
It applies to:
single hulled crude oil tanker and/or bulk carrier vessels built before 1 January 2002;
double hulled or semi-trailer vessels built after 1 January 2003;
bulk carriers built before 31 December 2004;
cargo ships built after 31 December 2005;
motor vessels built between 1 January 2006 and 31 December 2008;
seagoing ships built between 31 December 2009 and 1 January 2010;
multi-trailers built between January 2011 and 31 January 2012.
EU countries are free to adopt their own national rules for the design, construction and age of their SOHO-compliant ships.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 20 July 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Union is a party to MARPOLPOLPOL 73/78 (see summary).
For more information, see:
Maritime safety and pollution from ships (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Single hulled vessel: a vessel designed and built to carry crude oil.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (EU), Euratom of 20 July 2002 on the recast of Regulation (EU/2004/EC) on the prevention and control of pollution from Ships (Recast) (OJ L 183, 20.7.2002, pp. 1-2)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Regulations (EC, Euromoney) No 596/2008 and (EC), Euroméneur (EC).
Regulatory (ECEC) 2015/856 amending Regulation (Euratom) 2015-856 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the design of single-hollers and of the construction and operation of multi-wheeler vessels and amending Regulations (EC and Euratom).
Directive 2014/23/EU on the coordination of measures to protect human health and the environment in the event of accidents involving ships carrying crude oil (Directive 2015/24/EU).

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement of workers and their families in the European Economic Area (EEA)
Freedom of mobility of workers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the freedom of movement and employment of workers in the Economic Area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
  
It aims to ensure that EU workers have the same rights as nationals of other EU countries when seeking work in another EU country.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) 87/852/EEC.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that workers who wish to work outside their country of origin have the right to take up available employment in the territory of an EU country with the same priority as nationals.
The regulation does not apply to conditions relating to linguistic knowledge required by reason of the nature of the post to be filled or to vacancies for which there is no vacancy in the EU country in which the job is being filled.
National workers may not be excluded from taking part in the management of bodies governed by public law and from holding an office governed by the public law of that country. They must enjoy the same social and tax advantages as national workers.
Where a worker wishes to move, he or she must be informed of living and working conditions and be regularly informed of the living, working, working and social aspects of the job.
When a worker moves, he/she must be given the same treatment as a national worker.
If a job is offered to him/her outside his/her country of birth, nationality or place of residence, the same conditions apply.
He/she is entitled to the same benefits as nationals in that apply to nationals of the country in question.
Vacancies
The European Commission must publish a list of services which can be used by workers wishing to move to find work. These services must forward to the services of the first EU country the details of suitable applications.
Each EU country must send to the Commission the list, drawn up by common accord, of services referred to in the regulation and any amendment thereto, and any amendments thereto, in the Official Journal of the European Union.
A Technical Committee composed of representatives of the EU countries and chaired by the Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s single market for goods and services is based on the principle of equal treatment for workers in different EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘Movement of workers’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Foreign worker: a person who is a national of a non-EU country but who is resident legally in another country for a period of time.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2004 of 21 December 2004 on the right of movement, employment and social security of workers (codified version)

Summary 111:
Controls on the production, import and export of controlled substances
Controlled substances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the control of substances used as feedstock
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that substances used for animal feed and animal feed production are only used for essential laboratory and analytical purposes.
It recasts Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 696/2003 on controlled substances.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all substances used or placed on the market for feedstock. It does not apply to substances used in animal feed or animal feed additives.
The legislation does not cover substances used to manufacture, process, transport, manufacture or transport foodstuffs.
EU countries may not export controlled substances to non-EU countries.
Exports
The European Commission must notify the EU country of the country of destination of the controlled substances for which they are to be exported.
Importers must inform the Commission of:
the quantities of such substances to which they intend to export;
the name and address of the consignor or the place where the controlled substance is to be imported;
a description of the quantities to which the substances are intended;
any quantities of the substances to be destroyed;
details of the type of products or equipment to be used;
information on the quantities and methods of destruction and the technology used for the destruction.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that:
in the event of the export of a controlled substance to an EU country, the country concerned must send the relevant data to the Commission;
at least 2 months before the export, including the date on which the substance may be exported;
on the date of receipt of the data;
within 3 months of the expiry date of the importation, the consignment must be accompanied by a copy of the relevant documents.
Export
The Commission must also notify the importing country of any quantities of controlled substance that may be used for export.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMATERIAL APPLY?
   
The Regulation has applied since 31 December 2004.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Feedstock’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Feedstock: a substance used to make animal feed.
ACT
  баркацашка (recast)
Decision 2004/696/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 31 October 2004 on the regulation on the controls on the manufacture, import, transport and use of substances intended for feedstuffs (Recast) (OJ L 183, 31.10.2004, pp. 1-2)

Summary 112:
European Union Environment Agency (EU EEA)
European Union EEA (European Union Environmental Agency)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the European Union Agency for the Protection of the Environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1082/2002 which had previously been amended several times.
It aims to ensure that the EU EEA plays an active role in the protection of the environment.
KEY POINTS
The European Union Eureka Network is the EU’s main source of information on the state and future state of the European environment. Its main tasks include:
collecting, analysing and analysing data on the present and foreseeable state and pressures on the environment;
monitoring and evaluating the impact of human activities on the environmental situation;
developing and disseminating information to the public and the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Commission;
providing advice to the European Commission on the implementation of EU environmental policies and programmes.
The EEA is responsible for:
the collection, analysis and dissemination of environmental data;
the analysis and evaluation of the impacts of human activity on the EU environment; and
the assessment of the effects of human actions on the future state and the future situation.
In addition, the EEA contributes to the creation of a network of national, regional and international organisations, such as the United Nations Environment Programme, the World Health Organization and the World Meteorological Organisation.
FROMBudget
The regulation lays down the rules for the operation of the EAE, including the structure, structure and structure of the management board and the budget.
Management
The management board is composed of representatives of the Member States, the Commission and the EU countries. It is chaired by the Executive Director. The Executive Director is appointed by the Management Board for a renewable term of 5 years. The term of office is renewable once and may not be renewed more than once.
Each Member State must designate one or more representatives of its territory as Director-General Executive Director of the Agency. This designation may be renewed if the Member State considers that the work of the Directorate-General of the EU Environment Agency is no longer necessary.
A management board of representatives from each Member State appoints an Executive Director to carry out the day-to-day running of the agency. The director-general of the Joint Research Centre (JRC) is the director of the joint research centre. The Director-general is assisted by a Scientific Advisory Committee.
Eureka is funded by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the Cohesion Fund. The EEA has a budget of €2.5 billion for the period 2014-2027.
Budget and expenditure

general budget: €1.8 billion for 2014-2020;
general expenditure: €3.2 billion for 2015-2026;
maintenance: €4.7 billion for 2016-2028;
administrative and infrastructure: €2 billion;
programmes: €5.7 million for 2017-2025;
research: €8 million for 2018-2030;
exchange of information: €6 million for 2019-2031;
environmental impact: €9 million for 2020-2035;
impact assessment: €10 million for 2030-2040;
climate change: €11 million for 2050-2050;
protection of the marine environment: €12 million for 2040-2060;
sustainable development: €13 million for 2025-2070;
preventing and mitigating adverse effects of climate change;
pollution: €15 million for the 2040 period;
traffic: €7.5 million for transport;
energy: €0.3 million for energy;
food: €500 million for food;
water: €700 million for water;
biomass: €800 million for agriculture;
other: €250 million for forestry;
marine resources: €750 million for fisheries;
land use: €600 million for aquaculture;
natural resources:€1 billion for forestry and fishing;
fisheries: €400 million for fishing and forestry, €1 million for livestock;
wildlife: €200 million for hunting and fishing, and €1,000 million for wild fauna and flora;
migratory birds: €150 million for birds;
aquaculture: €100 million for fish;
seaweed: €50 million for oysters and molluscs;
tourism: €60 million for shellfish and shellfish;
animal health: €30 million for marine mammals and reptiles;

Summary 113:
Accreditation and licensing of environmental organisations
Accreditation/licensing of organisations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the accreditation of organisations and the licensing of organisations in the field of environmental compliance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the establishment of accreditation* and licensing* bodies for environmental organisations.
It aims to improve cooperation between the European Union (EU) Member States and the European Commission to ensure that environmental organisations are accredited and licensed.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU Member States. It does not apply to the European Economic Area (EEA).
It applies to the EU’s Member States, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland, Norway and Switzerland, as well as the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) and the United Kingdom (1) (1).
Rules for organisations
Organisations must be accredited and/or licensed by an accreditation body or by a competent body.
They must comply with the rules laid down in the regulation.
These rules include the following.
The organisation must:
register with the competent body or body;
provide all the necessary documents and information;
be open to the public;
have the necessary financial resources to carry out its activities;
ensure that the organisation complies with the requirements laid down by the regulation;
report to the competent bodies every 3 years on the activities of the organisation;
keep a register of all the information they do;
apply for accreditation and licence;
notify competent bodies if they consider that an organisation no longer meets the requirements of the regulation or that it no longer fulfils certain requirements;
implement an environmental impact assessment procedure.
Each organisation must submit a report on its activities to competent bodies and to the Forum of Accreditation and Licensing Bodies and the Committee referred to in the first subparagraph. These reports are to be made publicly available.
Registration
An organisation must be registered with a body accredited or licensed in the Member State in which it has its head office. This body is responsible for ensuring that the organisations comply with all the requirements set out in this regulation. The organisation must also ensure that:
the competent bodies carry out their tasks correctly;
the organisation has the necessary resources and expertise;
there is no conflict of interest between the organisation and competent bodies;
environmental verifiers are independent and impartial;
organisations are required to ensure compliance with the regulation and that their activities do not adversely affect the environment;
they must ensure that their environmental impact assessments are carried out in accordance with the relevant legislation.
In addition, organisations must ensure:
that their activities are based on sound environmental principles;
that they respect human, animal and plant health and safety standards;
their activities are environmentally sound and that they respect the principles of sustainable development and environmental protection;
where appropriate, the organisation must cooperate with competent bodies.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the environmental performance of organisations. These include:
Decision No 1600/2002/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 22 July 2002 laying down the Sixth Community Environment Action Programme on the improvement of collaboration and partnership with enterprises as a strategic approach to meeting environmental objectives.
For more information, see:
‘Cooperation and partnership’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Accreditation body: an organisation that has been accredited, licensed or certified by an accredited body or competent body to ensure the compliance of its activities with environmental requirements.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2004 of 20 December 2004 on the establishment, on behalf of the Community, of an Accreditation or Licensing Body or Competent Body for environmental compliance (OJ L 183, 31.12.2004, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 550/04 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 114:
Rules on the definition, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks and on the protection of geographical indications of certain spirit drinks (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Rules on:
the definition, presentation, presentation of and labelled of spirit beverages
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It lays down rules on the description, description and presentation of, and the labelling, of spirits.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to spirit drinks produced in the EU. It does not apply to spirits produced in non-EU countries.
It does not cover:
alcoholic spirits;
liqueurs and liqueurs intended for human consumption;
wine or wine fortified for distillation or by the redistillation of a wine distillate at less than 86 % vol., natural botanicals provided that the juniper taste is predominant, or
the mixture of the product of such distillation and ethyl alcohol of agricultural origin with the same composition, purity and alcoholic strength;
natural and/or nature-identical flavouring substances or/or flavouring preparations as specified in category 20(c) may also be used to flavour distilled gin.
EU countries are responsible for the regulation’s implementation and for ensuring that it is applied uniformly throughout the EU and that it does not infringe on the rights of producers, importers, distributors and retailers.
Labelling
The label must contain the following information:
a description of the spirit drink;
an indication of the country of origin;
the name of the producer and the country where the drink is produced.
The words ‘natural botanical name’ or ‘traditional production method’ must be written in a language easily understood by the consumer.
An indication must also be written on the label of each spirit drink.
Where a spirit drink is sold in a bottle, the label must include the name and the geographical indication ‘European spirit drink’. The label must also contain the information ‘EU’, ‘national’ and ‘country of origin’ in the same format as the national label.
In addition, the regulation requires the label to contain a list of the ingredients used to make the drink and the number of hectolitres of alcohol per litre of the drink, as well as a description of each ingredient.
Directive 2014/35/EU amended Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the labels of spirit-based alcoholic drinks (see summary). This amended the regulation to take account of the fact that some spirit drinks may not comply with the rules laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to modernise and simplify the rules on spirit drinks. These include:
introducing a new category of spirit drink (‘wine-based liqueur’) and a new definition of ‘mixture’;
establishing a European geographical indication (EGI) for spirit drinks;
protecting certain geographical indications (e.g. ‘Himbeergeist’).
For more information, see:
‘Spirits’ on the European Commission's website.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down common rules to protect persons and goods within the European Union (EU) against acts of unlawful interference with civil aircraft that jeopardise the security of civil aviation.
KEY POINTS

The legislation does not apply to non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1) and the United States of America (2).
It does not affect the EU’s right to fly to the United Nations or the International Civil Aviation Organisation (ICAO).

Summary 116:

Targeted restricted measures against certain persons and entities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 — targeted restrictive measures
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent and combat serious violations of human rights and abuses by freezing funds and economic resources of natural or legal persons, entities or bodies responsible for, providing support to, or otherwise involved in serious violations or abuses.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to individuals, entities and bodies listed in Annex I of the regulation, as well as those associated with them.
It does not apply to the EU’s general budget.
EU countries must take all necessary measures to ensure that:
the individuals and entities covered by the regulation do not benefit directly or indirectly from the freezing of funds or economic resources;
the measures are effective, proportionate, dissuasive and dissuasive;
there is no possibility for them to benefit from the frozen funds, economic resources or assets;
they are subject to a travel ban, asset freeze or asset freeze.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of this regulation.

Summary 117:
European Union military operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFOR IMMEDIATE RELEASE
SUMMARY OF:
Council Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 — European Union military crisis management operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes and launches a European Union (EU) military operation to contribute to preventing arms trafficking within its agreed Area of Operation and Area of Interest in Libya.
KEY POINTS
The EU’s mission is to:
contribute to the capacity building and training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy;
provide technical assistance to the Libyan Armed Forces and the Libyan National Police;
support the efforts of the United Nations Support Mission in Libya (UNSMIL) and the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY) in the fight against the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDs);
ensure compliance with the arms embargo on Libya, including UNSCR 1970 (2011) and subsequent Resolutions (UNSCR 2292 (2016) and UNSCRs 2473 (2019).
The decision authorises the European Union Military Crisis Management Operation (EUVFOR MED IRINI) to: provide technical assistance and assistance to Libyan authorities, the UNSMIL, the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and INTERPOL;
collect, store and exchange with the relevant authorities of Member States, competent EU bodies, the United States of America, the relevant UN bodies, INTERPOL, the ICCTICTY and the UN Support Mission to Libya;
assist in the training of Libyan coast guard and navy;
facilitate the transfer of personnel, equipment and equipment;
controllage of Libyan vessels and personnel to the EUNAVetting procedures for possible trainees;
organise and monitor the training and deployment of Libyan Coast Guards and Navy personnel;
prepare for the deployment of EUVessels to the Mediterranean;
monitor the proper execution of the EUVFORCE FOR MEDIRAINI
The European Union Maritime Crisis Management Organisation (EUMC) is the primary point of contact with the EUMC. The EUMC is responsible for:
monitoring the operation;
coordinating and coordinating the EU's response and coordination with other EU and non-EU Member States;
ensuring that EU vessels are diverted to ports designated in the EUNaval Crisis Management Operations Plan.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
On 31 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
On 19 January 2020, a Conference was held in Berlin on Libya. The High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy, Federica Mogherini, presented a proposal for the EU to establish and launch a Mediterranean military operation.
For more information, see:
‘EU’ on the European External Action Service's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Mediterranean crisis management operations: a military operation designed to prevent and combat trafficking in arms and related materiel.

   Council Decision CFSP/2020/472 of 31 March 20th 2020 on a European EU military operation on the Mediterranean [Official Journal L 101 of 4.4.2020].

Facilitating and combating document fraud
SUMMARY OF:

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the FADO system, which is designed to facilitate and combat document fraud.

The European Union agency for the operational management of large-scale IT systems in the area of freedom, security and justice, the European Commission, is responsible for the operation and management of the system.

KEY TERMS
Authentic documents: authentic documents are genuine documents that have been produced by the competent authorities of an EU country.
Falsified documents: false documents are pseudo documents, documents which have been forged and counterfeited.
MAINFORMATION: documents which are not authentic but which have the appearance of authenticity.

Summary 119:
Regulation of the profession of professions — common rules for the protection of workers’ rights and the freedom to conduct a business
Regulation (EU) 2017/1369 — common standards for the regulation of professions
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/33/EC on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the regulated professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ national laws on the regulation and supervision of professions are consistent and harmonised across the EU.
It lays down rules on the different ways in which EU countries may regulate professions.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all professions, whether they are regulated by national legislation or by EU-wide rules.
EU countries must ensure that:
the legislation is justified and proportionate to the nature, the content and the impact of the provision;
the measures are proportionate;
there is no discrimination based on sex, race, colour, religion, ethnic origin, age, disability, sexual orientation, age of the worker or sexual orientation;
employers’ representatives must be informed of the legislation and the reasons for its application.
The European Commission must facilitate the exchange of information between EU countries on the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The regulation applies to the professions listed in Directive 2005/36/EC (see summary).
For more information, see the European Commission’s website on regulated professions (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Employer: a person who has a legitimate interest in a profession.

Summary 120:
Management of by-catch stocks in the EU and its Member States by 2020 (Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the management of fish stocks
Management of species-by-catch (MSY)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on the EU’s management of fisheries by 2020
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to tackle overfishing, illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing and destructive fishing practices, and to implement science-based management plans, in order to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time feasible, at least to levels that can produce MSY as determined by their biological characteristics.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
fisheries in the Mediterranean, Black Sea, Atlantic Ocean and Black Sea;
fishing in the Black Sea and Atlantic Ocean;
the Black Sea in the Baltic Sea and the Atlantic Ocean in the North Sea; and
the Atlantic Ocean, the Caribbean and the Mediterranean.
It does not apply to stocks of Atlantic salmon, tuna, swordfish, porpoises and swordfish.
By-catch
The regulation lays down rules for:
the management of stocks of fish caught in mixed fisheries;
management of stocks that have been overfished, illegally caught or unreported;
landing obligations;
regional cooperation;
monitoring of stocks;
data collection and analysis;
scientific and technical measures;
restrictive measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYields
The legislation applies from 1 January 2015.
.
BACKGROUND
The EU countries must ensure that:
bycatch stocks are managed according to the best available scientific and technical information;
stocks are managed in a way that ensures that they do not overfish;
there is no risk that stocks will fall below levels that are sustainable for the long term;
target stocks are maintained at levels that do not threaten the long-term survival of the species.
For more information, see:
Fisheries bycatch (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* By-catch: fish caught by the fishing industry, such as sharks, rays, skates and skates, that are not caught in the wild.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 122:
Declaration of cash and disclosure of cash: preventing and deterring the diversion of illicit proceeds to finance illicit activities
Declaration, disclosure and control of cash
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the declaration, disclosure, control and confiscation of cash, and on the exchange of anonymised risk information and risk analysis results
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that cash is declared and disclosed to the competent authorities of EU countries to prevent and deter the illicit diversion of money to finance illegal activities.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to cash:
prepaid cards (i.e. cards issued by a bank or other financial institution);
cash issued by the European Central Bank (ECB), the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) or the European Union (EU) Anti-Money Laundering Office (EUAFRICOM).
It does not apply to cash issued by credit institutions, such as banks, credit unions, insurance companies or credit unions.
Declarations and control
Declarantes of cash must declare or disclose cash to the authorities of the EU country in which they are located. They must also make the cash available for control and, if necessary, to present it to those authorities for control.
The competent authorities must also be able to confiscate cash that is not declared or disclosed.
Defenders of the right of access to cash
Defendants must make a declaration of the amount of cash they hold in their bank accounts. They should also make a copy of the declaration.
They must also provide the authorities with:
the name and address of the bank or credit union where the cash is held;
the date on which the cash was received and the name of the person who issued the cash
the amount of the cash and the date of receipt
the identity of the individual who issued it.
In addition, the authorities and the European Financial Investigation Unit (FIU) of the European Commission must:
identify the persons and companies who have the right to hold cash in their accounts;
assess the risk of cash being used for illicit activities;
make a declaration on the cash held in their account.
If the authorities believe that a person who holds cash may be involved in a criminal offence, they must confiscate the cash. If they suspect that the person in question is not a national of an EU country, they may also confiscate or seize the cash in question.
Where cash is seized, the declarant must inform the competent authority of the country where it was seized and inform the authorities in that country of the reason for the seizure.
A declaration must be accompanied by:
a statement of the nature of the offence
a description of the circumstances in which the money was obtained and the circumstances under which it was obtained
a list of the persons responsible for the offence and the persons who are responsible for its recovery.
An endorsed copy of a declaration must also accompany the declaration
The declaration must include the name, address and contact details of the holder of the prepaid card and the person responsible for issuing the card.
Prepaid cards are easily transferrable and can be used to transfer considerable value across the external borders.
Exchange of information
EU countries and the Commission must ensure that:
all information exchanged between competent authorities and with the Commission is anonymised;
personal data obtained by operation of the regulation is processed only in a manner compatible with the purposes of this regulation;
all personal data are stored for a period of 5 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATOR APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Prepaid card: a prepaid card issued by an international financial institution or by a credit institution.
* Cash: cash issued in the form of a credit card, a debit card or a prepaid debit card. Such prepaid cards can be easily transferred between the EU countries and with non-EU countries.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2004 of 21 December 2004 on the Declaration, Disclosure and Control of Cash, and the Exchange of Information and Risk Analysis Results

Energy Union – energy security and energy efficiency
SUMMARY OF:

ensure energy security; the internal energy market; energy efficiency; decarbonising; and research, innovation and competitiveness.

energy security;the internal market;energy efficiency;decarbonisation;research and innovation; andresearch and development (R&D).
The energy strategy is based on a number of objectives, including:
reducing greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions;
improving the efficiency of energy production and consumption;

promoting sustainable jobs;

Summary 124:
Waste and waste-related substances — Regulation (EC) No 596/2008
Waste — Regulation(EC) no 596 / 2008
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 on waste
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the disposal of waste containing substances listed in Annex III of the Convention on the Law of the Sea (COSMOS Convention).
It aims to ensure that waste does not release substances that are hazardous to human health and the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all waste, whether it is solid, liquid, paper, plastic, paperboard, wood, wood pulp, paper and paperboard.
It does not apply to:
waste from incineration, incineration or incineration;
placing on the market and use of articles already in use containing endosulfan (PFOS); or
products containing or contaminated by any substance listed in the annexes to the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that:
all waste is disposed of in a manner that minimises the release of the substances in the form of incinerated waste;
the total releases of substances are recorded in inventories;
there are national action plans on measures to identify, characterise and minimise, with a view to eliminating where feasible as soon as possible, the total releases.
The European Union Agency for the Prevention of Pollution from Waste (EPA) is responsible for the management of waste.
In addition, the European Commission is assisted by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSAFAO).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECAST APPLACE INTO FORCE
   
The Regulation entered into force on 10 July 2008.
BACKGROUND
The Convention on Waste and Waste-Related Substances is the cornerstone of the EU’s waste policy.
For more information, see:
The EU Waste Framework Convention (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Waste: waste consisting of, containing, contaminated by, or consisting of waste that has been incinerated.
* Endosulfur hexafluoride: a substance that is toxic to humans, the environment and the food chain.
ACT
Council Decision 2008/500/EC of 25 June 2008 on waste and waste related substances (Recast)

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic packaging — EU rules on plastic waste and waste prevention and recycling (SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/1369 on plastic packaging
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to protect the environment and human health from the adverse effects of plastic packaging on human health and the environment.
It is part of a package of measures designed to tackle plastic waste in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all packaging materials, including composite materials, which are intended to be used just once or for a short period of time before being disposed of. It does not apply to single-use plastics.
EU countries must take into account the relative properties of different packaging materials and their life cycle assessments and design for circularity.
The legislation does not cover:
tobacco products with filters containing plastic;
single-use products intended for short-lived use;
plastic packaging intended for use in short-term applications;
towards the disposal of waste from post-consumption waste of tobacco products with filter containing plastic.
Implementing measures
EU governments must ensure that:
all packaging materials are designed to be reused, recycled or composted;
all waste is collected and disposed of in an environmentally sound manner;
there is a separate collection system for tobacco products that are not intended for re-use or recycling;
it is possible to set up separate collection systems for tobacco-related waste from other packaging materials that are intended for long-term reuse or recycling.
In addition of plastic waste to landfishing vessels and waste from fishing vessels is prohibited;
the EU countries must establish separate collection and recycling systems for waste from non-hazardous waste from the fishing vessels and landfills.
Information systems and reporting
Each EU country must set up a system for collecting and reporting on the results of its waste collection and collection systems. This system must be based on the best available information and must be available to the public and the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The plastic waste directive is one of a series of measures aimed at tackling plastic waste. The other measures are:
the plastic packaging directive (Directive 94/62/EC), which aims to reduce plastic waste by 20 % by 2020;
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on plastic-based packaging (see summary);
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 652/2013, (EC), (EU, EFTA) No 1069/2013 and (EC).
For more information, see:
Plastic waste (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Single-use packaging: a plastic product which is intended for a single use and which is not designed for reuse, recycling or composting.
* Combating plastic waste: a material which is used only once and for a very short period before being thrown away.
ACT
Council Directive 2009/23/EC of 20 December 2009 on plastic and plastic packaging (OJ L 183, 31.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
Regulating (EC)(EU) No 796/2012 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 December 2012 on plastic, plastic packaging and plastic waste (Oj L 183/2012, 31st of December 2012, p. 1)
Regulate (EC(2014) No 1282/2013 of the United Nations Environment Programme on the protection of the environment from plastic waste, amending Council Directive 94/64/EC and repealing Council Directive 93/65/EEC (Official Journal L 347, 29.11.2013).
last update 14.02.2018



Summary 126:
European Union Intellectual Property Office (EU IPO)
European Union IPO (EUIPO) — combating intellectual property infringement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the protection of intellectual property
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect intellectual property rights (IP) against infringements by companies and individuals.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) 782/2002 which had been amended several times.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the Benelux Office for Intellectual Property. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
The EUIPO is the EU’s central intellectual property office (IPO).
It is responsible for:
protecting intellectual property against infringement by companies, individuals and associations;
collecting, analysing and enforcing intellectual property claims;
providing information on infringements;
ensuring that EU countries’ national laws are applied to infringements of IP;
monitoring infringements and ensuring that the relevant national laws and regulations are applied correctly.
In addition, the regulation provides for the creation of an EU-wide register of infringements. This allows EU countries to keep a record of all infringements that have taken place in the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
