Summary 127:
Exceptions to copyright and related rights for the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain works and other protected subject matter
Exceptions and limitations to copyright in the field of accessible formats
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — exceptions and limitations for making and distribution of copies in accessible format of certain protected subjects matter
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations* to copyright or related rights when making and disseminating copies, or for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
copies of protected works, such as books, films, television programmes, music, software, etc.;
the making of accessible format copies of protected material;
the exchange of these copies across borders.
It does not apply to the making or distribution of the following:
books, films and television programmes;
audio recordings;
computer software;
sound recordings.
Exclusions and limitations
The contracting parties are responsible for ensuring that:
the content of the accessible format copy is protected by the relevant copyright, related rights or equivalent rights;
there is no limitation on the use of the protected material in a way that is contrary to the public interest;
it is made in a language easily understood by the public.
There is also a requirement for the authorisation of the authorised entity, beneficiary persons or rightholders.
EU countries must ensure that the competent authority of the EU country in which the copyright is protected, or the country where it is registered, has the necessary information to carry out the necessary checks.
The competent authority must also:
monitor the implementation of the regulation;
take the necessary measures to ensure the proper functioning of the system;
develop guidelines or best practices on making and distributing accessible formats;
provide information to the competent authorities of EU countries and the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Accessible formats (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барканска граца бълака, гаранка  глашка (OJ L 94, 1.3.2017, pp. 1-2)

Mutual assistance in the fight against widespread infringements with a European Union dimension
Mutual aid in combating the spread of illegal drugs
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/829 on the application of Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 on the mutual assistance in combating widespread infringement with a Union dimension (‘Mutual Assistance Regulation’)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities and the European Commission to combat drug trafficking.
It lays down rules for the application and enforcement of this regulation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to drug trafficking with a EU dimension. It does not apply to infringements that have a national dimension. However, the effects of such infringements are not limited to a single EU country.
EU countries are required to cooperate in the investigation and prosecution of drug trafficking offences and to cooperate with the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and the Single European Liaison Office (SOLAF).
The European Commission is responsible for coordinating the actions of EU countries in the field of combating drug trafficking within the EU.
Rules for mutual assistance
The competent authorities concerned by the alleged widespread infringement must cooperate with OLAF and the single liaison office to investigate and prosecute the alleged infringements. The competent authorities must cooperate in order to ensure that:
the infringement is brought to justice;
the penalties are effective and dissuasive;
there is a clear and proportionate penalty for the infringements; and
the offender is held liable.
The relevant to the infringement are proportionate to the seriousness of the offence.
Where the infringement is alleged, the competent authorities should take enforcement measures based on the outcome of the investigation.
If an EU country fails to comply with a request for assistance, the EU country concerned must inform the other EU countries and the Commission within 1 month of receiving the request.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle drug trafficking in recent years. These include:
Directive 2014/23/EU on combating drug-trafficking (see summary) and Directive 2014/24/EU (EU Anti-Trafficking Directive).
For more information, see:
Drug trafficking (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Drug trafficking: the trafficking of drugs by means of trafficking in human beings.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 829/2017 of 21 December 2017 on the exercise of the right of mutual assistance between Member States’ competent authorities in criminal matters and on the prevention and investigation of infringements of the laws of Member States relating to drugs.
REFERENCES

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices (IVMDs) — EU rules on safety and health
In vitro diagnostics (IVD)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/35/EU on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to in vitro diagnostic devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to harmonise the rules on the sale, manufacture, import and use of in vitro (IVDM) medical devices.
It is part of a package of measures to modernise the EU’s rules on medical devices designed to diagnose, monitor and treat diseases.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all devices designed and manufactured for the diagnosis, monitoring, diagnosis and treatment of diseases. It does not apply to medical devices intended for use in hospitals, laboratories, universities, research institutes or other institutions.
Manufacturers and importers must ensure that:
the devices comply with the requirements laid down in the directive;
they meet the essential safety requirements;
the device complies with the relevant EU legislation;
there is a written declaration of conformity and a technical documentation.
Importers and distributors must provide the relevant authorities with the necessary information to ensure that the devices meet all the essential requirements.
The devices must be accompanied by:
an electronic system on performance studies;
a technical documentation;
proof of conformity;
an EU declaration of compliance.
An electronic system to monitor the devices’ health and safety;
certification of the device and its components.
a notification of conformity to the competent national authority.
the manufacturer must provide a copy of the CE conformity assessment report to the European Commission.
EU countries must notify the competent authority in each EU country of their decision to approve or reject the device.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
In 2015, the European Parliament and the Council adopted Regulation (EU) 2015/852 on the European Medicines Agency (EMAA).
For more information, see:
‘In vitro-diagnostics’ on the EEA's website.

Summary 130:
EU rules on the sale and putting into service of electrical appliances and their fittings (Directive 2009/142/EC) (Official Journal C 202/2009).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It lays down rules to ensure that electrical appliances sold in the European Union (EU) comply with the European Commission’s technical harmonisation and conformity assessment rules (TFEU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
electrical appliances (e.g. fridges, freezers, washing machines, pressure cookers, dishwashers, etc.).
It does not apply to: appliances designed and manufactured for the production of food and beverages;
appliances intended for use in the home;
electromagnetic compatibility (EMC) appliances;
circuit-circuit breakers;
countersigns.
Manufacturers and importers must ensure that:
the appliance or fitting bears the CE marking before it is placed on the market;
the CE marking is affixed before the appliance or the fitting is placed into service;
there is a single point of contact at which the manufacturer can be contacted.
Importers and distributors must check that the product complies with the requirements laid down in the directive before placing it into service.
The manufacturer is responsible for ensuring that the appliance and/or the fitting comply with all the rules laid down by the directive.
If an appliance or a fitting is found to be in breach of the directive, the importer must inform the competent authority of the EU country of origin and inform the manufacturer of the reason for the non-compliance.
EU countries may impose penalties for serious infringements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLACE INTO FORCE?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
 
The legislation is part of the ‘new approach’ principles, as set out in the Council Resolution of 7 May 1985 on a new approach to technical standards and harmonisation.
For more information, see:
‘Electromagnetic Compatibility’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Directive 2010/104/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2010 on the harmonisation of technical standards applicable to electrical appliances (OJ C 202, 12.12.2010, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Directive 2010/106/EU have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

  (1)

Summary 131:
Cross-border transport of euro cash: cash-in-transit (CIT) and cash-carried out by police in the EU (until 31 December 2020)
Transport of euro banknotes and coins by cash-transport companies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 — cross-border cash transport
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the security of euro-denominated cash in the European Union (EU).
It lays down rules for the transport of cash by CIT companies (cash-carriers) between EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the transport arrangements for the cash-transfer from one EU country to another.
It does not apply to:
cash-transporting operations carried out by the national police forces of the EU countries;
transport operations involving the police of the host EU country;
high-value point-to-point transport by the police; or
cash transport by police of a non-EU country.
CIT vehicles must be equipped with a global navigation system (GNSSN) and a device that allows them to be tracked at a remote distance by police;
the police must be notified to the police in advance of the operation.
EU countries must ensure that the CIT vehicle is accompanied by a security staff and that the vehicle is equipped with weapons and ammunition.
National police forces may carry out inspections of the vehicle during the transport. Such inspections may also be carried out in the host country’s territory.
The Commission may decide to grant prior authorisation to CIT to carry out such inspections.
FROMBanks, the large retail sector and other professional cash handlers should be able to contract with a cash-handling company that offers the best price and/or service and to take advantage of the cash services of the nearest national central bank (NCB) branch or CIT cash centre, even if it is located in another EU country. The Commission must decide whether to grant such a contract within 72 hours of receipt of a request from the bank or the retail sector.
In addition, the Commission may authorise CIT vehicles to be accompanied by police officers from the police force of the country in which the operation is being carried out. If the police are present, they must be informed of the nature and duration of the transport operation and of the security measures to be taken.
Where cash is transported by a CIT company, it must be transported under the authority of a national police force or by a police officer from the country where the operation takes place. The police may also take part in the operation if they consider that it is necessary to protect the safety of the public, the economy or the national security.
A CIT security staff must be trained in the use of weapons, ammunition, explosives and other weapons and must be provided with the necessary training and equipment to ensure that they are used only in the event of a serious threat to public order or security. In addition, national police officers must be given the necessary equipment and training.
When a cash transport operation is carried out for the purpose of the safekeeping of cash, the police must carry out a risk assessment to determine whether it is possible to detect, prevent or detect the presence of cash. If they believe that there is a risk that cash may be stolen, they may take the necessary measures to prevent the cash from being taken or to confiscate the cash.
If the police believe that the cash is being transported for the purposes of a criminal offence, they should inform the national authorities and inform the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Commission.
This regulation is without prejudice to the application of national rules that require:
national police forces to be notified of cash transport operations;
national authorities to be informed in advance;
custodians of cash to be armed;
cash to be transported by police to ensure the safety and security of the police and the security staff;
vehicles to be fitted with a GPS system;
carriers to be escorted by police.
BACKGROUND
The CIT sector is one of the most important sources of cash for the euro area and is a key provider of services to the banking sector. CIT is also a major source of revenue for the EU as a whole.
For more information, see:
‘CIT’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cash-carrier: a company that transports cash by road, rail, air or sea.
* CIT: a transport company that carries out cash transport by road or by air.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2006 of 29 June 2006 laying down rules on the cross-Border transport of euros and coins (OJ L 183, 30.6.2006, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
Council Decision (EU) 2016/852

Food labelling — labelling and labelling of foods and foodstuffs (EU rules on labelling) (Directive 2014/35/EU)
Food labeling and labeling — labeling of foods, foodstuffed animals and animal feed
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/34/EU on food labelling
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules to ensure that food business operators provide the European Commission with the information it needs to ensure the safe and wholesome food it sells.
It also aims to improve the accessibility to the visually impaired.
KEY POINTS
Food business operators must provide the Commission with:
a mandatory declaration of the country of origin of the food;
a list of ingredients;
an indication of the storage conditions or conditions of use.
The declaration must be written in a language easily understood by the public. Where space does not permit, the declaration must appear in linear format.
Food businesses must ensure that the declaration is accompanied by:
the name of the business;
the date and place of production;
where appropriate, a description of the product;
information on the labelling or presentation of the ingredients.
EU countries may require food businesses placing on the market in their territory foods bearing this information to notify the competent authority of the EU country where the food originates.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATED DIRECTIVE APPLY?

KEY TERMS

Summary 133:
EU rules for the issuance of euro coins
EU rules on the issuance and retention of euros
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1210/2010 — rules for issuing euro coins and collector coins
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down binding rules to ensure that euro coins are issued and kept in circulation throughout the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU countries may issue two types of euro coin: circulation coins and collectible coins.
Issuance of circulation coins:
circulation coins must be issued and put into circulation at face value.
Collector coins may be put on the market at or above face value and may not be used as a means of payment.
The European Central Bank (ECB) approves the volume of coin issuance to be approved by the European Commission.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that they take all appropriate measures to discourage the use of collector coins as payment. These measures include:
preventing the circulation of coins which are not euro coins unfit for circulation within the meaning of point (b) of Article 2 of the regulation;
implementing measures to prevent the sale of coins that do not meet the requirements of this regulation.
Consultation prior to the destruction of circulating coins
The Commission must consult each other via the relevant subcommittee of the Economic and Financial Committee and inform the mint directors of the EU countries whose currency is the euro.
Implementation of Regulation (EU countries must submit a report to the Commission every 2 years on the implementation of the regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE
The regulation has applied since 26 June 2012.
BACKGROUND
The Regulation is one of a series of measures designed to modernise and simplify the EU’s legal framework for the single currency.
For more information, see:
‘Issuing euro coins’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Coin: coins issued in denominations of 1- and 2-cent coins and commemorative coins with a common design.
* Collector coin: coins that are minted for the purpose of collecting.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  
Commission Implementing Regulation (EC) No 1182/2010 of 26 June 2010 laying down rules for:
the issuance and keeping in circulation and retention in circulation of the euro coins;
collector coins: coins minted by national mints for the purposes of collecting coins issued by other EU countries.

Summary 134:
European Social Fund (ESF) — mission, scope and investment priorities (until 31 December 2020)
European Society Fund — mission and scope
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — the EU’s European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the mission and the specific investment priorities for the European Society Fund, the European Union's (EU's) social fund.
It also lays down specific provisions concerning the type of activities that may be financed by the ESF.
KEY POINTS
The ESF has a mission to:
promote social inclusion, gender equality and equal opportunities;
encourage social inclusion and gender equality;
support transnational cooperation and mutual learning;
contribute to the development of human capital and employability skills.
The regulation sets out specific rules for the specific types of activities financed by ESF, such as:
research and development (R&D);
training and mentoring;
transnational cooperation;
education and training;
equitable access to education;
research, training and mobility;
exchange of best practice;
employment and social inclusion;
training of social workers and social workers;
access to education and training.
Funding
EU countries must ensure that:
the ESF's investment priorities are consistent with the EU's general investment priorities;
they are based on a coherent, growth-oriented human capital development strategy;
there is a specific investment priority for each investment priority;
the priorities are identified by the European Commission;
based on a common output and result-oriented investment strategy.
Eligibility for ESFinance
The European Commission is responsible for managing the European social fund and the managing authorities of an operational programme in a region defined in Article 90(2)(2)(a) or (b) of the regulation or in a Member State eligible for support from the Cohesion Fund (see summary).
The managing authorities are responsible for:
monitoring the implementation of the investment priorities and the results achieved;
assessing the impact of the investments;
ensuring that ESF investments are compatible with the general EU investment priorities.
In addition, the managing authority must:
ensure that the investment priority is aligned with the common investment priorities identified in the European Pillar of Social Rights;
provide the Commission with all the information it needs to make a decision on the allocation of ESF resources;
identify the priority areas where ESF funding is to be made available to non-governmental organisations (NGOs);
assess the results of the activities supported;
make recommendations to the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council on how ESF funds should be allocated.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.

total population for specific EU purposes;

deaths, marriages and divorces;

Summary 136:
EU rules on the labelling and labelling of fibre composition of textile materials (until 31 December 2020) (until the end of 2023)
EU controls on the quality of fibre content of textile material
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 — labelling, labelling or marking of fibres used in textile materials
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It lays down rules to ensure that products containing fibres are labelled and labelled in accordance with the requirements laid down in Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 on the composition of fibre.
It aims to protect consumers from false information on the fibre content and to ensure the proper functioning of the EU’s single market.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
textiles intended for human consumption;
fibrous materials intended for use in clothing, tableware and tableware;
goods and household articles intended for sale to the public;
new textile products intended for personal use;
old made-up textile products, where explicitly stated to be such.
The legislation does not apply to certain fibres, such as polypropylene, polyethylene, polyester, polyvinyl chloride (PVC) and vinyl chloride monomer.
Labelling
Textile materials may be labelled with the following information:
the fibre composition;
the name of the manufacturer or importer;
where appropriate, the date of manufacture and the place of sale;
a description of the fibres and the method of manufacture;
information on the origin of the fibre;
in the case of new products, the type of fibre used;
an indication of the production process and the name and place of the place where the fibre was obtained.
Where a product is labelled as being made up of more than one fibre, the information must include the fibre composition and the number of fibre fibres per kilogram of the product.
If a product does not comply with the legislation, the product may be subject to a penalty of up to €10 per kg or €20 per tonne for each kilogram.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE APPLY?
The regulation applies from 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Fibre composition labelling (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Fibre composition: the percentage of fibre in a product compared to other fibres.
ACT
Council Directive 2008/853/EC of 29 November 2008 on the classification of fibre-rich textile materials and repealing Council Directive 2004/96/EC (OJ L 347, 31.12.2008, pp. 1-8)

Summary 138:
Insurance-based investment products for retail investors — rules
Insurance–related investment products (i.e. investment products)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on retail investment products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to ensure that retail investors have access to investment products that are suitable for them and that they can make informed investment decisions.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all insurance-related products offered to retail investors.
It does not apply to insurance-based insurance products that do not provide investment solutions or that are combined with insurance coverage or that can be complex and difficult to understand.
The legislation does not cover investment products which are not covered by insurance or which do not comply with the EU’s general rules on investment products, such as life insurance, pension funds, annuities, life insurance and mutual funds.
In addition, the directive does not affect investment products offered by the European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority (EIOPA).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
This directive is part of a package of measures designed to protect retail investors from the risks associated with investment products.
For more information, see:
‘Retail investment products’ on the European Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Regulation (EC) No 552/2014/EU
1.

- KEY TERMS
- Insurance-related investment product information document
- Retail investor information document (PRIIPIC)
- Investment product calculators
PRIIPSIC-related product information sheet
PRICELISA-related information sheet (PIC-R)
PRICE
The retail investor must be provided with:
—
a PRICELINE (a key information document containing information on the investment product and the investment strategy and the risks related to the product, including a description of the product and its risk profile, and a summary of the risks and benefits associated with the investment, and the risk profile of the retail investor, including the product type and investment strategy, the investment risk profile and the product risk profile;
--
a PICER (a product description and risk profile) and a PRICER-related document (a description of how the product is designed to be sold, the product features and risks, etc.).
The regulation also lays down rules on the use of electronic communications (e.g. electronic communications, e-mail, telephone calls, etc.) to ensure the transparency of investment products and that the information provided to the investor is easily accessible and understandable.
PRIGELINE
The PRICE DOCUMENT
The Retail Investor Information Document (REIT) is a document that provides information on retail investors’ investment options and investment strategies. It should be provided in a language easily understood by retail investors and should be accompanied by an accompanying electronic communication.
This document should be sent electronically to the consumer within 3 months of the date on which it is received. However, retail investors should be given the option to receive it on paper.
When a retail investor receives a PRIIP document, they should be informed of the terms and conditions of the transaction and of any other information they may need to know in order to make an informed investment decision. Retail investors should also be given a choice of the medium in which the document is provided to them, allowing for use of the electronic communications where appropriate having regard to the circumstances and the transaction.
If the PRICE document is not provided electronically, the investor should receive a copy of the document on paper or, where appropriate, an electronic communication with a link to the document.
Procedures and penalties
The European Commission may impose penalties on the retail investors who fail to comply with this directive. The Commission may also impose penalties if it considers that they are disproportionate to the risk or that the product does not meet the requirements of the legislation.
Amending Directive (EU) 2019/852
The new rules introduced by Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1139 amends the directive to take account of the fact that some retail investors do not have the necessary financial resources to carry out their investment activities. This means that the regulation introduces a new type of penalty for those who do not respect the conditions laid down in the regulation. The penalty is calculated on a case-by-case basis and may include a reduction in the amount of the penalty or withdrawal of a retail investment product from the market or a suspension of the sale of that investment product.
Implementing Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2018
This regulation introduces new rules on retail investor information sheets and investment calculators. These are designed to help retail investors to make investment decisions more easily and quickly.



Summary 139:
Resolution of failing banks and other insolvent entities
Resolving failed banks
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — resolution of failing banking entities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the resolution process for failing banks in the European Union (EU).
It lays down the principles and procedures for the resolution of failed banks.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all entities that have failed or are likely to fail within the EU.
It does not apply to banks that have been insolvent for more than 6 months.
The resolution process is based on a number of principles, including the following:
the failure of the entity must be recognised as a failure;
the resolution process must be carried out within 6 months of the failure of an entity;
investments must be sufficiently sectorally, geographically and proportionally diversified;
equity claims should not be guaranteed;
no guarantee should be guaranteed for equity claims;
proceeds should be calculated net of the costs arising from the failure and from the resolution.
Early intervention
EU countries must notify the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Commission within 24 hours of a bank’s failure. The notification must be made not less than 24 hours before the measures are to take effect.
Resolution procedures
The ECB and the Commission appoint a resolution board. The board is responsible for the implementation of the regulation. It is chaired by the Commission and is composed of representatives of the EU countries. The ECB is assisted by the European Banking Authority (EBA). The Commission is also participates in the process.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE
 
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
  
For more information, see:
‘Resolving insolvent banks’ on the ECB's website.

Summary 140:
Noise-related airport noise management — rules on aircraft noise management
Airport noise management: rules on noise-related airports
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 on noise management of airports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the soundness of air traffic at EU airports and to protect human health and the environment from the adverse effects of noise.
It lays down rules on the management of noise at airports.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all airports in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to airports in non-EU countries.
EU countries must designate one or more competent authorities responsible for the process to adopt operating restrictions and for the implementation of these restrictions.
Rules on the introduction of operating restrictions
Aircraft operators and airports must take the necessary measures to minimise the impact of noise on human health, the environment and the safety of passengers and crew. These measures must include:
the withdrawal of marginally compliant aircraft from the airspace;
measures to reduce the noise impact over a given period of time, i.e. over 3 years;
the use of noise-mitigating measures;
cost-effective measures to reduce noise impact.
Noise management measures must be in place to:
eliorate the impact over the 3-year period;
ensure that aircraft operators comply with the noise management requirements;
protect the health of passengers;
prevent noise-induced damage to the environment;
improve the safety and security of crew and passengers; and
protect public health.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
airport facilities and infrastructure are equipped to cope with noise impacts;
noise-mitigation measures are put in place;
airports are equipped with sound-absorbing noise-absorption equipment;
there is an adequate number of aircraft and aircraft operators are trained and equipped to deal with noise;
environmental and health aspects are taken into account in the design of noise mitigation measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Noise’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Noise: the noise emitted by aircraft during the flight from an airport to an airport.
ACT

Cross-border insolvency proceedings: cooperation and communication between insolvencies practitioners and courts and judicial authorities
Cross-Border insolventcies: cooperating and communicating with courts and judges
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 on cross-border proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the application of the European Union’s (EU) rules on insolvent proceedings.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries where the debtor has an establishment. It does not apply to cases where a debtor does not have an establishment in one EU country.
It does not cover:
cases in which the debtor is a national of another EU country;

Where proceedings are opened in the EU country of establishment, the proceedings are governed by national law. However, proceedings may be opened in other EU countries if there is a likelihood that the debtor will not be able to continue to exercise his or her business activities or the debtor's business activities.
In addition, the regulation provides for the establishment of a coordinator to act as a single point of contact between the debtor and the courts in the country where proceedings are to be opened. The coordinator is responsible for:
coordinating the proceedings;
exercising the rights of creditors;
providing assistance to the debtor;
ensuring that the creditor's rights in rem are respected.
Cooperation and communication
between the parties involved in the proceedings and between the courts and the parties concerned.

Summary 142:
Compulsory insurance for passengers in maritime accidents
Compulsion insurance for passenger accidents
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 on compulsory insurance for damage caused to passengers in the event of a maritime accident
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the protection of passengers when travelling by sea.
It lays down rules for compulsory insurance under the Athens Convention on maritime accidents.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the liability of the carrier and the performing carrier of a passenger ship or a vehicle accompanying a vehicle or live animals which are covered by a contract for the carriage of goods not governed by this convention.
The liability rules are based on the principle of a global limitation of liability. Insurance companies must only be liable for their part of the damage caused, while the carrier is not liable for the loss or damage caused by the negligence of the passenger.
Insurance companies must:
provide passengers with a certificate of insurance or other security to show that they have insurance;
ensure that the insurance is sufficient to cover the total amount of damage caused;
notify passengers of any damage to their luggage or to their personal property;
keep a list of the places of business where the insurance and other security is established;
inform passengers of the place of business and of the type of insurance they are entitled to receive compensation.
A State Party may authorise an institution or an organisation recognised by it to issue a certificate to issue the certificate.
Compulsary insurance must be provided that:
the liability is limited to:
damage caused by an accident caused by a passenger;
loss or damage to luggage;
damage or loss to personal property caused by damage to a vehicle, live animals or goods not covered by the carriage contract;
damages caused to the vehicle or to live animals.
EU countries must ensure that the certificate is issued by an institution recognised by the State Party to the Convention and that it is accompanied by a copy of the relevant national law.
In addition, EU countries must: ensure that insurance companies provide passengers with information on the type and nature of the insurance they have provided;
make sure that the information is provided in the most appropriate format;
use the most up-to-date information available;
take all necessary measures to ensure that passengers are provided with the information they need to make an informed decision on whether to claim compensation for damage or loss caused to their property.
Liability of carriers and performing carriers
Carriers and the carriers’ performing carriers are liable only for damage to passengers.
Performing carriers and their performing carriers may not be held liable for damage that is caused by negligence on the part of their passengers. The liability burden is on the claimant to prove fault or neglect on their part.
Defendants may also be liable if they fail to take the necessary preventive measures to prevent damage to the passengers’ personal property. In such cases, the claimant may seek compensation from the carrier.
Where damage is caused to a passenger’s personal property, the defendant is liable only if the damage was caused as a result of negligence by the carrier or the performing carriers. The carrier is also liable if it was negligent when it failed to provide the passenger with the necessary information to make a decision on the insurance.
Persons who have been injured or who have lost their property are also liable. However, the burden of proof lies with the claimant if it is proved that the negligence was due to the negligence or fault of the claimant.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
  
For more information, see:
‘Athens Convention’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Directive (EU, Euratom) 2016 / 852 of 29 June 2016 on compulsory liability for damage causing to passengers (OJ L 183, 1.7.2016, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  (1) The United Kingdom withdraws from the European Union and becomes a third country (non-EU country) as of 1 July 2020.
  The United States withdraws as a third party from the United Nations Security Council in 2019.
last update 14.02.2020

Summary 144:
Road transport operators’ professional qualifications
Road Transport Operational qualifications
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2010 — common rules for the qualification of road transport operators
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all road transport undertakings in the European Union (EU) have the same level of professional qualifications.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all undertakings which engage in the occupation of the transport of goods and passengers by road.
It does not apply to undertakings established before 1 January 2011 and which have been established since 1 January 2012. It does not cover undertakings set up after 1 January 2013 or established after that date.
To be eligible for the regulation, an undertaking must meet the following criteria:
be established by a person with a good repute in the field of transport;
have sufficient financial resources to cover the costs of running the undertaking;
be based in an EU country other than that of which the regulation applies;
not be established in a non-EU country.
Applicants must have the following documents:
a bank guarantee or a professional liability insurance;
a professional competence certificate;
an undertaking’s annual accounts and annual accounts.
The applicant must also provide the competent authority with the following information:
the name and address of the company;
the number of vehicles and the number of employees;
procedures for setting up and operating the undertaking.
A transport manager must be appointed on a permanent and continuous basis by that person, and indicates his or her responsibilities as transport manager.
In addition to the above information, the competent authorities must check that the undertaking complies with the requirements of the regulation.
If an undertaking is found to be in breach of these requirements, the undertaking must be wound up or liquidated.
Penalties for infringements
The penalties provided for are effective, proportionate and dissuasive. They include:
suspension of the licence of the undertaking or the transport manager;
restrictions on the use of certain road haulage vehicles and tolls and infrastructure user charges;
temporary or permanent disqualification from the road transport sector; and
restriction of the right to use certain road passenger transport services.
Examination and registration of applications
The competent authority must examine and register all applications and ensure that they comply with the rules laid down in this regulation. The competent authority may also refuse to accept an application if it considers that the applicant does not meet the requirements laid down by the regulation or if the applicant has not complied with the conditions laid down.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2010/96/EC has applied since 4 December 2010 and had to become law in the EU countries by 4 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of transport services provided by road transport companies, such as:
rules for establishing and operating undertakings;
rules on the licensing of drivers and drivers’ representatives;
requirements for the certification of transport managers;
conditions for the recognition of professional competence.
For more information, see:
‘Road transport’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Road transport operator: a person who operates a road transport vehicle, a bus, a coach, a semi-trailer, a van or a trailer.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2010 of 4 November 2010 laying down common rules on the qualifications and professional competence of road Transport Operators

Summary 145:
Beneficiaries of social security benefits — cooperation between social security institutions
Benefit claimants — cooperation with social security authorities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the application of the basic regulation on the social security system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries’ social security systems to ensure that beneficiaries of benefits are able to access their rights as quickly as possible and under optimum conditions.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to persons covered by Article 11(3)(d) of the Basic Regulation on the Social Security System (see summary) who are unemployed or self-employed and who reside in an EU country other than that of their place of residence.
It does not apply to:
unemployed persons who resided in a Member State other than the competent EU country when they were subject to the basic Regulation;
persons covered by Regulation (EU) No 1283/2003 on the eligibility of benefits and pensions;
employed persons whose last activity was outside the EU country in which they lived when they received the benefits;
people covered by the Directive on the conditions of employment and social security in respect of their last activity as an employed or self employed person.
The legislation applies to all EU citizens and nationals of the Member States, except Denmark, Ireland, the United Kingdom (1) and the United States of America (1).
It applies to the following the death of an EU citizen.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY
It has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
The basic rules
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to implement the regulation, including:
Directive 2014/23/20/EU on social security rules for the period 2014-2020;
Delegated Directive 2014/24/EU, which aims to simplify and streamline the procedures for social security claims in the case of death, disability and long-term care;
Decision 2014/22/EU (EU, Euratom) 2016/852 on the implementation of the regulation;
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/25/EU/856 on the coordination of measures in the field of employment, social security and pensions.
MAINFORMATION
The social security regulation applies in all Member States except Denmark.
For each Member State, the competent national social security institution must:
provide the claimant with all the information necessary to calculate unemployment benefits which can be obtained in the Member State of residence, in particular the salary or professional income received;
make available to the claimant all the relevant information necessary for calculating unemployment benefits, including the date at which the benefits were paid;
notify the claimant within 3 months of receiving the benefits and inform him/her of the reasons for the rejection of the claim;
inform the claimant of the measures to be taken by the institution to which the claim has been rejected;
ensure that the claimant is given all the necessary information to calculate the benefits.
In addition, the regulation provides for:
a procedure for withdrawing benefits if the claimant has not been able to prove that he/she is no longer entitled to them;
a process for revoking benefits if:
the claimant has been unable to prove his/her unemployment benefits; or
the debtor institution has rejected the claims for a relevant reason within 3 years of the date on which the claims were made;
the creditor institution has not paid the cash benefits within that period.
EU countries must ensure that:
all information provided by the claimant must be entered into the relevant national database;
all relevant information must be provided to the institution of the country of residence at the request of the national institution of which the claimant lives;
information must be exchanged between the national authorities and the institutions of other Member States;
there must be coordination between the authorities and institutions;
any action taken by national authorities is recorded in the national database.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2008.

Summary 146:
Data collection and dissemination of health information and statistics
Data collection & dissemination of Health information & statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 establishing a Community statistical programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) statistical programme to collect and disseminate health data and statistics.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the rules and procedures for the implementation of this regulation.
It aims to develop a sustainable health monitoring system with mechanisms for collection comparable data and information, with appropriate indicators, and to develop, with the European Statistical Programme (Eurostat), the statistical element of this system.
The European Commission is responsible for collecting and disseminating the data. It is assisted by Eurostat, which collects and disseminates the statistics for the EU’s statistical programme.
EU countries are responsible for the collection of the data and for the dissemination of the statistics. The Commission is assisted in this task by the European Monitoring Centre for Health Statistics (EMCS).
The Commission has the power to adopt implementing measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 			
The EU has been developing a sustainable, comparable and reliable health monitoring and statistics system for the European Union since 2008.
For more information, see:
‘Health statistics’ on the European Commission's website
’.
MAINFORMATION
KEY TERMS
Health monitoring system:
European Union statistical programme:
Eurostat is the EU's statistical programme for health statistics. It collects and distributes statistics on the EU countries' health systems and on the basis of national and regional health systems.
Health statistics:
statistical data on the prevalence, prevalence, incidence and prevalence of diseases, and the causes of death;
health-related data: data on physical and psychosocial health problems, such as work-related health problems and illness;
deaths: deaths caused by causes of disease or by accidents;
sickness: deaths due to accidents or injuries;
work-related illness: deaths caused by illness or accidents caused by work;
social epidemics: deaths related to social exclusion from the labour market, unemployment or social exclusion;
traffic accidents: traffic accidents, road accidents, fires, explosions, floods, etc.
Social epidemiological data: these are collected from existing population surveys such as the European Health Interview Survey (EHIS) or other social surveys.
In addition, the Commission collects data on social epidemics such as social exclusion, unemployment and social exclusion.
National authorities must collect data on all EU countries’ health systems, including on the number of people who are unemployed or underemployed, and on health-related problems and illnesses.
Data must be collected in the framework of the WHO International Classification of Diseases and must follow Eurostat rules and the UN and WHO recommendations for population statistics. In addition, national authorities must ensure that the data are collected in line with the relevant EU statistical rules.

Summary 147:

EU statistical data on job vacancy — quarterly data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1882/2003 — EU statistical requirements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down EU-wide requirements for EU countries to provide the European Commission and the European Central Bank (ECB) with quarterly data on short-term changes in job vacancies.
It is part of the EU’s statistical programme for the period 2009-2013.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the Commission with data on the number of vacancies in the labour market in each EU country by the end of the third quarter of each year.

Eurostat must publish the data on a quarterly basis on the Commission's Internet site.
Each EU country must submit the data to Eurostat on the basis of a request from the Commission. The Commission must assess the quality of the data transmitted and ensure that they comply with the statistical requirements.
Every 3 years, the Commission must publish a report on the quality and reliability of the statistical data provided by the EU countries.

Summary 148:
Hybrid hydrogen-powered vehicles — type-approval and safety requirements for hydrogen fuel containers
Hybrid fuels
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — type approval and safety standards for hydrogen-power vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the design, manufacture, testing and certification of hydrogen fuel storage containers and systems.
It also lays down requirements for the installation of such components and systems and for the identification of the vehicles.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles designed and built for the transport of hydrogen;
motor vehicles;
battery-powered electric vehicles; and
hydrogen-powered light-duty vehicles (e.g. heavy-duty trucks, buses and coaches).
It does not apply to certain types of hydrogen storage containers or systems, such as those used to store compressed hydrogen.
The regulations also lay down requirements on:
the installation of the hydrogen storage container;
the hydrogen fuel supply lines;
conditions for the declaration of compliance with the regulation’s safety requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive (EU) 2017/852 amends Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2004 with regard to hydrogen-fueled vehicles. It applies from 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to encourage the development and use of hydrogen vehicles. These include:
rules on the type of vehicle and its components;
rules for the certification;
requirements on the installation and testing of the vehicle;
measures to ensure that the vehicle complies with the requirements of the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Hybrid vehicles’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Hydrogen fuel storage container: a container designed and constructed to store hydrogen and containing hydrogen fuel.

Council Regulation (Euratom) 2018/856 of 21 December 2018 on type approval, testing, certification and the information provided by manufacturers, importers, distributors and retailers of hydrogen-based vehicles (OJ L 150, 24.12.1.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 149:
Natural gas transmission networks — rules for the internal market in natural gas (Directive 2009/73/EC)
Natural gas Transmission Networks — conditions for access to the natural gas market
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2009/72/EC on the conditions for the access of transmission system operators to natural gas transmission systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It lays down minimum requirements for the operators of the European Union’s (EU) transmission networks.
It aims to create an internal market for natural gas.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision applies to the transmission networks of all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland. It does not apply to the EU countries’ natural gas pipelines.
The regulation applies to all natural gas distribution networks in the EU.
Requirements for operators
Transmission system operators must operate their networks in accordance with EU network codes. Operators must ensure that they comply with the requirements laid down in Regulation (EC) No 1775/2005 on conditions for market access to gas networks (see summary).
Operators must also ensure that:
the transmission system operator complies with the rules laid down by the European Energy Agency (EAA);
the EAA is independent of the national regulatory authority;
transmission system users have the right to participate in the decision-making process;
the regulation does not hamper the entry of new market participants and does not create undue barriers to market entry;
all transmission system users are given the right of entry into and out of the transmission system.
to participate in market surveillance and market surveillance.
Rules for market surveillance
The EAA must:
be independent of national regulatory authorities;
ensure that the EAA does not interfere with the functioning of the market;
take into account the integrity of the system concerned and the security of supply;
be transparent and accountable to the public;
not discriminate against network users;
provide the public with all the information they need to make an informed decision on the operation of their system;
apply the rules set out in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The directive is part of a package of measures designed to create a single market for gas in Europe.
For more information, see:
‘Natural gas’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: a natural gas system operator responsible for the transmission of natural gas into, out of, into or out of a gas distribution network.

Summary 150:
Agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries on civil matters
Agreements involving EU citizens
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on the application of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union to agreements between EU and non EU countries on matters of civil matters (Civil Cooperation Regulation)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU-non-EU agreements on matters relating to civil matters are not incompatible with EU law.
It seeks to ensure the proper functioning of the EU’s external relations policy.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must notify the European Commission of their intentions to open or continue formal negotiations on an agreement with a third country on matters falling within the scope of the regulation.
The Commission may participate as an observer in the negotiations between the EU country and the third country as far as matters falling under the regulation are concerned.
In order to conclude the agreement, the Commission must assess the expected impact on EU law of the outcome of the negotiations and, if necessary, request the inclusion of particular clauses in the envisaged agreement.
Notification to the Commission
The EU country concerned must inform the Commission of its intentions with a view to obtaining an authorisation to open formal negotiations or to conclude an agreement. It must provide all relevant information and documentation enabling the Commission to assess whether the agreement would render EU law ineffective and does not undermine the system established by that law, or undermine the functioning of that law. The Commission must also provide any other additional information.
Authorisation of negotiations
If the Commission may propose negotiating guidelines and may be included in the agreement. If necessary, it may also request specific clauses.
When the Commission decides to authorise the conclusion of an agreement, it must provide a copy of the agreement to the European Parliament and the Council of the Member State concerned. If the Commission considers that the agreement does not comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation, it can refuse authorisation.
If a Member State considers that an agreement envisaged by the Commission does not render Community law ineffective, it is obliged to inform the Council and the Parliament of the reasons for this decision and to submit a report on the decision to the latter.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 13 July 2013. EU countries had to notify the Commission by 13 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Judicial cooperation in civil matters’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 12 July 2013 on the approximation of the laws of Member States and of third countries on the exercise of the right of access to justice in matters of judicial cooperation between Member States (OJ L 183, 13.7.2013, pp. 1-2)

Summary 151:
Statistics on asylum and migration
Statistics on migration and asylum
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 311/76 on the collection and analysis of Community statistics on Asylum and Migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality of EU statistical data on asylum, migration and international protection.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the rules for the collection, analysis and transmission of EU statistics on migration, asylum, international protection, residence permits and residence of third-country nationals.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) 311/75.
The purpose of the regulation is to reinforce the exchange of statistical information between EU countries and the European Statistical System Office (ESSO).
The aim is to ensure that EU statistics are collected and analysed in a reliable, timely and harmonised manner.
EU countries are required to collect, analyse and transmit statistics on:
the number of asylum applications and applications for international protection;
refugees and asylum seekers;
the total number of persons who have been refused entry to the EU;
international protection and the number of people who have received international protection and who are in danger of being returned to their country of origin;
information on the number and types of asylum seekers who are staying in the EU.
Data collection and transmission
The data collected and transmitted must be in the form of national statistics. These statistics must be disaggregated by age and sex, and by the citizenship of the persons concerned. They must be available to the European Commission and to the national authorities.
the Commission must ensure that the data are collected in the appropriate formats and that they are transmitted to the competent national authorities in the relevant EU countries.
Statistics
The Commission must collect and analyse statistics on the following categories of migrants:
persons who have applied for asylum and who have not been granted asylum;
people who are seeking international protection or who are on the verge of being granted international protection but who are unable to reach the EU because of their physical, mental or financial situation;
persidents who are asylum seekers and who do not have the necessary documentation to prove their asylum status;
individuals who are refugees;
migrants who have entered the EU illegally;
non-EU nationals who are not nationals of an EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATOR APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the world’s largest single source of migration statistics.
For more information, see:
Migration statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Asylum and migration: a term used to describe the conditions under which a person is considered to be at risk of being a danger to his or her own safety or to the safety of others.
* International protection: the protection provided by the International Organization for Migration (IOM).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 309/76 of 20 December 2007 on the Collection and Analysis of Community Statistics in the field of Migration and Asylum (OJ L 183, 31.12.2007, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, No 309 /76 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 152:
Public-interest audit — statutory audit
Public–interest audit: statutory audit — public-interest function
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/829 on the performance of statutory audits
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the public interest function of statutory audit is respected by EU countries’ auditors and audit firms.
It lays down rules on the independence of the statutory auditor and the audit firm.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland. It does not apply to the European Union (EU).
It does not affect the role of the European Commission or the European Court of Auditors (ECB).
The statutory auditor is responsible for the audit of the EU’s financial statements.
Auditors must respect the independence and independence of statutory auditors. They must also respect confidentiality and professional secrecy rules.
They must not provide non-audit services.
The audit report must be in clear and unambiguous language. It must contain all relevant information, such as:
the statutory audit report;
the results of the audit; and
the audit firm's annual report.
A statutory auditor or an audit firm must submit to the audit committee an additional and more detailed report on the audit results. This report must include:
a description of how the audit was carried out;
a summary of the work carried out and the results;
details of the reasons for the decision-making process;
any changes to the statutory audit;
information about the audit procedures;
procedures to be followed by the auditor or audit firm;
an explanation of the reason for the change in the audit procedure.
In addition to the regular dialogue during the carrying out the audit, it is important that both parties:
regularly exchange information on the work they have done;
meet regularly to discuss the audit process and the outcome of the dialogue;
have a regular and detailed discussion on the results of their work;
keep each other informed of any changes they have made to their audit procedures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYORK INTO FORCE?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Public interest audit’ on the European Agency for Quality Assurance's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2009/979/EC of 20 December 2009 on the implementation of the public–interest statutory audit (OJ L 183, 31.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Directive 2009-979 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes the European Producer Organisation (EPO) which is responsible for managing the markets for the marketing of fish, shellfish and crustaceans in the EU.
The EPO is an association of producer organisations recognised in one or more EU countries.
It is a non-profit-making organisation whose main objective is to organise and implement measures to improve market surveillance and market surveillance.
Producers must submit a production and marketing plan for at least one of the main marketed species to their competent national authorities for approval.
Each producer organisation must submit an annual report to the European Commission on the implementation of the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that the EPO has the necessary resources and expertise to implement the regulation and that it has the legal capacity to carry out the tasks it has been assigned to it by the Commission.
Member States must cooperate with the Commission to ensure that producer organisations comply with the requirements of this regulation. The Commission may, for example:
the EPOblame producers for the failure to comply with this regulation;
producer organisations may be subject to fines;
the Commission may impose penalties for infringements of the rules laid down in the regulation; and
the regulation does not apply to producer organisations established before 29 December 2013.
In addition, the Commission may authorise the Member State concerned to impose sanctions on producer organisations that do not comply with these requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?

KEY TERMS
* Producer organisation: an organisation established by a producer organisation for the purpose of managing the market for a particular species of fish or shellfish.
* Market surveillance: the surveillance of the production, processing, storage and marketing of products by producers in order to ensure the proper functioning of markets for that species.

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 — EU securities settlement system rules
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) securitisation system (CSD) is open to all participants in the securities settlement process.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules on the regulation’s implementation.
It applies to all CSDs, whether or not they are regulated by the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) or the European Banking Authority (EBA).
It does not apply to participants which are CCPs (competitors of securities firms) or to CSDs which are not regulated by ESMA.
The CSDs must comply with the rules laid down in the regulation.
They must:
ensure that:
all CSDs comply with all relevant EU rules and regulations;
provide the relevant authorities with the necessary information and information on their activities;
operate in compliance with the relevant EU and national securities legislation;
notify the competent authorities of any failure to comply with their obligations under the regulation;
report to the Commission every 2 years on the implementation of the regulation, including the number of CSDs that fail to comply;
have the right of appeal against any decision taken by the Commission;
be transparent and accountable to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions and the European Central Bank of the EU.
EU countries must ensure that CSDs are subject to the same rules as other regulated entities, such as banks, insurance companies and other financial institutions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE INTO FORCE?

BACKGROUND

It is a system in which securities firms are responsible for the settlement of securities traded on a regulated market. It may be used to settle securities trades between securities firms and other investors.

Directive 2013/23/EU on the European Regional Development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
It aims to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-Border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
The ERDF is a European Union (EU) instrument designed to support regional cooperation (R&T) in the areas covered by the EU’s common development strategy (NUTS).
It is designed to:
contribute to the development of regions in the most disadvantaged areas;
improve the quality of life of people in the least disadvantaged regions;
promote regional integration and cooperation;
reduce disparities between regions in terms of economic and social development;
support regional cooperation in the fields of education, health and social protection;
encourage regional integration, education and training;
strengthen regional cooperation, particularly in rural areas.
The main objectives of the ERDF are:
to contribute to the creation of sustainable, inclusive and inclusive societies;
to reduce disparities between regional development levels;
the development of rural areas and the least benefited regions; and
to promote regional integration.
to improve the quality and sustainability of the lives of people, especially in the north and the north-eastern regions, in particular.
To achieve these objectives, the EU supports regional cooperation programmes in the following areas:
transnational cooperation programmes:
cross-border,
interregional,
regional cooperation,
cooperation in the field of health and education, and
cooperative and interregional development.
Cooperation between regions
The European Commission is responsible for managing and coordinating these programmes.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It entered into force on 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘The European Region Development Fund’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Regional development strategy: a strategy designed to improve the living conditions of people living in the less developed regions of the EU.
ACT

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the efficiency of the energy sector and to encourage investment in energy-efficient energy projects.
It sets out the criteria and procedures for the designation of priority energy projects by the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
The European Commission’s Directorate-General for Energy, Energy Efficiency and Energy Efficiency Agency (EDEA) is responsible for the design and implementation of the Ecodesign.
The regulation is part of the Europe 2020 strategy, which aims to achieve sustainable growth by promoting a more resource-efficient, more sustainable and more competitive economy.
This regulation aims to ensure that energy efficiency is a key element of this strategy.

the creation of a common list of priority projects;
the establishment of regional lists of projects with a common interest; and

* Common interest: a project which has a significant environmental, social or economic impact and which is likely to have a significant economic impact.

Summary 160:
Complaints against traders — Online Dispute Resolution (ODR) platform
Complaints on traders’ websites
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — Online dispute resolution (ODRD)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It lays down rules for the use of online dispute resolution* procedures (ADR) to resolve disputes between traders and consumers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all disputes between consumers and traders where the relevant ADR procedures are offered by ADR entities listed in accordance with Directive 2013/11/EU.
It does not apply to disputes where disputes are initiated by traders against consumers where the ADR procedure is offered by an ADR entity that is not listed in the directive.
The ODR platform is an interactive website which can be accessed electronically and free of charge in all the official languages of the EU. It allows traders to submit complaints against consumers. It also allows consumers to lodge complaints against traders without having to go to court.
EU countries must designate at least two ODR contact points hosting at least 2 ADR advisors in each EU country.
ADR entities must:
ensure that the complainant has access to a complaint procedure;
provide the complainant with all the necessary information to resolve the dispute;
notify the complainant of the complaint within 3 months of receiving the complaint;
take all necessary steps to ensure that the complaint is dealt with in a timely and effective manner.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLACE INTO FORCE?
When the regulation came into force
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve consumer protection, such as:
Directive 2013/13/EU on consumer protection (Directive on consumer rights and consumer information protection) and Directive (EU, Euratom) 2017/1369 on consumer data protection.
For more information, see:
‘Online dispute resolution’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Dispute resolution procedure: a procedure where a trader submits a complaint to a third party (e.g. a consumer).
* ADR: an online procedure to resolve a dispute between a trader and a consumer.

Regulation (EU)-2018/858 amends Regulation (EEC) 2018-856 to ensure the protection of consumers in the event of unfair competition and to ensure a high level of consumer protection in the EU’s internal market. It introduces a new type of complaint procedure, ‘Out-of-court ADR’, which is designed to allow consumers to submit a complaint against a trader without going to court to settle the dispute in a dispute via the online ADR platform. It does not replace the existing dispute resolution procedures.
This regulation is part of a package of measures aimed at improving consumer protection and consumer rights in the internal market, which include:
protection of consumers’ personal data;
improving consumer rights;
ensuring that consumers have access to the information they need to lodge a complaint; and
protecting their privacy and the rights of their children and other persons who may be affected by a complaint.

The European Union’s Common Agricultural Policy (CAP) — Croatian direct payments (2014-2022)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules for the implementation of the basic payment scheme (CAP), which is the cornerstone of the EU's agricultural policy.
It also provides for the creation of a complementary national direct payments scheme for Croatia.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.

the creation of the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD);
the introduction of a basic payment system for Croatia;
the establishment of a national direct payment scheme.
KEY TERMS
* Basic payment scheme: a system of direct payments for farmers and small- and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs).
* Direct payments: direct payments are paid directly to farmers and SMEs based on their gross national income (GNI).

Direct payments for Croatia:
direct payments to farmers;

direct transfers to small SMEs;
cooperation payments to small businesses;
exchanges of information on the structure and functioning of the CAP;
transfers of entitlement entitlements;
transfer of payment entitlements between Croatia and the EU;
